---
or a

To be sure, I thought I was a touch nuts because o

But a
---

---

---

years, I 
---

shapes. At the same time, my relationship with my tools is like a tumultuous

combination o
---

---

sleep with and a care
---
ul gardener.
My wi
---

---

time I raised my voice in anger or became emotional in dealing with 
---
riends
and 
---
amily. But I did grab a table leg once and beat the living hell out o
---

paper-
---

and it still would only chew up my work.

On the other end o
---

that are so reliable that I have 
---
eelings 
---
or them that I should probably discuss with a therapist. These tools — a smoothing plane, a dovetail saw and
a combination square — have become worn in the places where I grip them.

They are always at arm’s reach when I build something, and they are the

tools I reach 
---
or to help me diagnose and 
---
ix problems when things go awry.
This book is the result o
---
 my experiences with tools 
---
or the last 50 years,

---
rom the time I acquired my 
---
irst coping saw at age 11 until the day I decided to sell o
---

o

---

And I hope that this story will help guide you in acquiring a set o
---

that will stick with you 
---
or the rest o

---

wield them, I hope you'll still wander down to your shop in the evenings and
lay your hands on their warm and worn wooden totes.
I chose the title o
---
 this book with care — it wasn’t the decision o
---
 some
cynical marketing department. Each o
---
 the three important words in the title
“The Anarchist’s Tool Chest” has a part in the story beyond this page. And
when you put the words together, my hope is that the result is greater than
the individual components.
The “anarchist” in the title is me. I dislike that word quite a bit, but it is
the right one. I hope to make the case that most woodworkers I've met are
“aesthetic anarchists” — people who work with their hands, own their tools
and seek to live in a world where making something (anything) is the goal
o
---
 each day.
Woodworkers generally labor alone, producing objects that are the result
o
---
 just our tools, our minds and our hands. And the objects that we build are

a slap in the 
---
ace o
---
 the chipboard crap that is 
---
orced down our throats at
every turn.

So though woodworking might seem a traditional, old-time skill, it is
quite radical in this consumerist age where buying stu
---

---
 is good and not buying stu
---

---
 is considered 
---
ringe behavior.

The “tool” in the title is the heart o
---
 the book. Tools allow us to shape the
world around us. But buying the wrong tools is a monumental waste that

could drain your bank account, slow your progress as a woodworker or even
sour you on the cra
---
t. In the last 14 years, I've studied and used more tools
than most people see in several li
---
etimes. Though that sounds like boasting,

it’s not something I’m particularly proud o
---
. And you don't have to make the
mistakes that I made.

And the “chest” is the logical result o
---
 the 
---
irst two words. A
---
ter realizing that I was an aesthetic anarchist and that I didn’t need every tool in the
store, I built a chest 
---
or the tools that I really needed (using the tools I really

needed) and stocked it with this essential set. I
---
 the tool doesn’t 
---
it in the

s a kid, my weekends consisted o
---

---

near our house and riding my bike to Ace Hardware and Sears to
ogle and 
---

I was, in short, obsessed with killing both all aquatic and deciduous li
---
e
in my neighborhood. And I would spend hours 
---

---
orbade me 
---

ways to catch 
---

Eventually I grew tired o
---
 
---

---
erred hangout

o
---

stores, on the other hand, were 
---

---

touch — tools that I coveted but couldn't a
---

---
ord.
Somehow one summer | scraped together enough money to buy a coping

saw. I 
---
retted (excuse the pun) over the decision because Ace Hardware and

Sears both carried coping saws, and I can remember traversing the parking

lot between the two stores as I made my choice. I settled on the Cra
---
tsman
coping saw.
What a piece o
---
 crap. I regret that decision to this day.

The saw is still in my basement, and it is still a shining symbol o
---
 garbage. It won't tension anything except my nerves. The blade rotates sicken-

ingly like a dislocated shoulder. But it does have a nicely 
---
inished hardwood

handle and a chromed 
---
rame.
This was my 
---
irst experience with what I like to call “tool-shaped objects” — things that look 
---
or all the world like tools but don't really do the
job required o
---
 them. At the time I should have tried to 
---
ix the saw with lock
washers, a welder or chewing gum. But instead I did something 
---
ar more

modern and stupid: I bought another coping saw.
It was the beginning o
---

their packages. And when they didn’t work I’d look 
---
or a di
---

---
erent tool that

would promise more. In other words: I tried to spend my way into good
cra
---
tsmanship. I ended up spending hours o
---

As a result, I have a lot

ive years ago. But
while I sold several piles o

as I spent 14 years amassing what I like to call “an enormous working set o

None o

I was your basic home woodworker. I was taking night classes at the Uni-

versity o

---

---

---
rom Walmart. A Black & Decker

---
rom the 1960s and 1970s
and were all chromed and weighed as much as a Christmas ham in the can.
But I didn’t know any better, so I liked my tools and the redolence o
---


their rotting electrical insulation.
That Sunday morning was a little di
---

---
erent than most. My wi
---
e and I had
decided the night be
---

Ohio, which is where she grew up. We had a 5-month-old girl who didn’t

---

So that morning I went to the grocery store and bought a copy o

The salary was a 30-percent pay cut. I immediately accepted it.
My 
---

really done. Until that point I had never seen a shaper, a spray booth, a hol-

low-chisel mortiser or an abrasive drum sander. I had never used a rip 
---
ence
on a table saw that actually locked square (Mr. Bill Biesemeyer became my
new best 
---

didn’t smoke or spark when it spun.
Almost every day, some new tool showed up in the mail 
---
or us to use. Let

me say that again so you might actually believe me: Almost every day, some
new tool showed up in the mail 
---

tools.
And this

---

keep them. But they do come, even sometimes i
---

tool person.” Here’s how ridiculous it can get: Once we wanted to test a new

biscuit joiner 
---
rom Porter-Cable. The public relations guy asked i
---
 we also
wanted to test the company’s new jigsaw. “Nah,” we replied. “We just need

the biscuit joiner.”
A 
---

---
rom Porter-Cable. It was the biscuit

joiner. And they had included two (two!) jigsaws to 
---
ill out the box. The
jigsaws were ballast.

To be sure, this massive 
---
low o
---

I never liked this set-up. So when I started reviewing hand tools

The net result is that I started amassing more hand tools than I (or anyone) needed. I ended up with several complete sets o

The Good Books

---

or example, mallets, smoothing planes and hatchets) and some tools seem
shared with the carpentry trade.
I
---

---
orward more than 150 years, not too much has changed. The

rom the tool list described by Moxon and

As the Industrial Revolution begins to crank out mass-manu
---
actured

tools, the basic list o
---

---
or basic joinery starts to expand.

There are more kinds o
---

---

(such as blocks, shoulders and routers), plus some new saws, including the

coping saw.
By the 20th century, the basic list o
---

---
or joiners stands at about 63,

according to books by Charles Hayward, the traditionally trained dean o
---


workshop writers. Still, when I looked at Hayward's list it seemed rather
paltry compared to what was in my shop. (See this book's appendix 
---
or a

comparison o
---

At 
---
irst, I attributed these short lists o
---

¢ Everything in the pre-Industrial age would have been more expensive
because it was made by hand.

¢ The general level o
---

¢ Technological innovation had yet to produce the 
---
antastic new tools
shown in the modern catalogs.
But all that was just denial kicking in.

Judging 
---
rom the descriptions o

ore mass production ruled the earth, there were two things going on that were related, but
that are easy

because the basic skill level was higher. Descriptions o

Anarchy 
---
rom a Woodworkers Perspective
I hesitated to use the word “anarchy” in the title o
---
 this book because it

means so many bad things to so many good people. In my high school, the
“anarchists” wore “Bad Brains” leather jackets, black make-up (that was the

boys) and had questionable hygiene.
They weren't

anarchists.

They called themselves

anarchists,

but they

knew as much about anarchism as they did about 
---
lossing.
Anarchy is the precise and correct word 
---
or my situation. And i
---
 you'll

bear with me, I think you'll understand why a boring guy 
---
rom the suburbs
who likes blue jeans and button-down shirts is a quiet anarchist.

For me, it’s quickest to explain what anarchy isn't: It’s not about violence,
the overthrow o
---
 governments, the dismantling o
---
 corporations or even the
smoking o
---
 a mild hallucinogen made 
---
rom boiling banana peels (actually, I
tried this. I don’t recommend it). Instead, anarchism is the realization that all

large institutions — governments, corporations, churches — have divided up
the tasks we do in our jobs to the point where these institutions do waste
---
ul,

dehumanizing and stupid things.
Eunice Minette Schuster states in the book “Native American Anarchism” that American aesthetic anarchy is “the isolation o
---
 the individual
— his right to his own tools, his mind, his body, and to the products o
---
 his
labor.”

LASTMAKERS

265

It's a desire to work 
---
or yoursel
---

and to run in social and economic circles

made up o
---
 other individual artisans.
Hey, that’s me. Heck, I have to believe that Schuster’s description applies

to most woodworkers | know. We generally labor alone, producing objects
that are the result o
---
 just our tools, our minds and our hands. These objects

are a slap in the 
---
ace o
---
 the cheap, mass-manu
---
actured termite-diarrhea 
---
ur-

niture in the discount stores. And we're proud o
---
 the 
---
act that our 
---
urniture
is better than the

stu
---

---
 
---
orce-
---
ed to
the masses.

Though
woodworking

might

seem

a

traditional,

old-

time

it is

skill,

rare and radical
stu
---

---
 in this age.

So when

all

these ideas came

into 
---
ocus in my

head, I realized
that I wanted to build 
---
urniture using 
---
ewer tools that were o
---
 good quality.
I wanted to build things that couldn't be bought in a store. I wanted to build
things requiring more skill than wallet. And I didn’t want to support the

system that encourages endless consumption.

All these utopian urges also made me realize that my shop was a miserable place to do all these things, especially compared to the shops drawn

in the early books such as Roubo’s, André Félebien’s “Des Principes de

LArchitecture” and Denis Diderot’s “Encyclopédie” (and those shops were
all probably sweatshops).

rom wood. They'll be longer in length and more accurate as a

technology and media companies, you're restricted in similar ways with the

And what i

to ask permission to make a long-distance call?

Since that job, I've always bought my own pens and notebooks. And I’ve
come to the conclusion that having my own

---

power tools, computers, whatever — is a good personal policy. So every tool

I discuss here is one I have bought with my own money.

So be

The conventional wisdom states that these planes are called “bench”
planes because they are always on (or right below) your workbench. I'll buy
that. When I work, I always have three bench planes close at hand: a 
---
ore/

jack plane, a try plane and a smoothing plane.
Bench planes are the tools that take wood 
---
rom a rough state and process

---

All bench planes do three things to a stick o

---

quick

---
 the blade is shaped and set correctly.
The try plane makes sur
---
aces as 
---
lat as possible. Some people call this

plane a jointer plane thanks to the nomenclature o
---
 the 19th century. I think
the term “try plane” is more apt. In early English parlance, a jointer plane

was designed to produce an edge that could be used to create an edge joint.
The slightly shorter “try plane” was used to make a wooden sur
---
ace “tried

and true.” Because “try” can apply to edges and 
---
aces, I pre
---
er the term “try.”
And smoothing planes are sel

smooth the wood somewhat, the smoothing plane produces a sur

ready 
---
or 
---

Wooden-bodied Planes: The body o

‘Transitional’ Handplanes
In the 19th century, planemakers made a style o

---

Transitionals have a hard time 
---

In essence, a transitional plane (Stanley didn’t use the word “transitional,” by the way) has a wooden sole and metal guts that adjust the iron. The

advantage is, o
---

phemer, have used a powered jointer on wonky examples. And you have the
precise depth and lateral adjustment o
---
 the metallic plane's iron.

But you give up some things in this wacky marriage.
Closing the mouth o
---
 the tool is a laughable exercise. There is usually a
partial 
---
rog (a tadpole?) that moves back and 
---
orth. Simple, right? Buzzzz
— Wrong! The metal moves, but the wood below it does not. So the iron
doesn't get the 
---
ull support o
---

---
rog sur
---
ace when you

The solution o

---

---

---

everywhere is too much work unless I need to stay warm living underneath

a highway overpass.
As a result, the transitionals can be di
---

---
icult to use when you want a 
---
ine

mouth and a 
---
ine 
---
inish. You can treat the plane's stock like a wooden-stock
plane and patch the mouth. Or you can buy a di
---

---
erent plane.

The last disadvantage o
---
 this 
---
orm o
---
 tool is that the rear totes tend to
come loose with ease. The solution is to add a screw to connect the bottom
o
---

iron 
---
rame).

Bottom Line: Making Them Work
Even with all the trade-o

---

on easy woods.

can achieve cutting angles higher than 45°, you'll have a plane that can deal

with reverse, curly or interlocked grain. We'll discuss these angles when we
get into the individual planes below.

The most versatile plane. The jack plane usually has a curved iron so it can hog o

Using the Jack Plane
Jack planes take a thick shaving — up to 1/16" i
---
 you can believe it. The trick
to this hog-tastic technique is to work across the width o
---
 a board. Not with

the grain. Not against it. But across it.
Working across the 
---
ibers allows you to take a bigger bite with less e
---

---
ort.
Why? Because you are cutting across the wood 
---
ibers (where they are weak)

instead o
---
 trying to lever them up and overcome their beam strength. This is,
in general terms, why crosscutting is generally easier then ripping.
I
---
 you are going to work across the grain, you do need to be cautious

about a couple things. First: The wood will “spelch” or tear out at the 
---
ar
edge o
---
 your pass. This splintering is ugly and can be severe. The way to
side-step this is to plane a cham
---
er across your 
---
ar edge. Two or three strokes
with a jack plane should produce a signi
---
icant cham
---
er and reduce spelching.

The second consideration is i
---
 the board’s sur
---
ace is cupped across its
width (like it usually is on the bark side o
---
 aboard) or bowed (like it usually

is on the heart side o
---
 a board). I
---
 you are working across a cupped sur
---
ace,
you should have an easy time. The jack will reduce the high edges until you

reach the lower middle ground.
I
---
, however, you are working the bowed side, the plane will tend to mde
over the hill in the middle and not make the board 
---
lat at all. It will merely

stay bowed. The solution is to work the center o
---
 the board only until the
center is in the same plane as the long edges. Or to even reduce the middle o
---

the board until it is slightly below the edges (cupped!). Then you can work

across the board — historically called traversing — to bring those high edges
into the same plane as the middle.

8 0

THE

ANARCHIST’S

TOOL

CHEST

A
---
ter you dress a sur
---
ace reasonably clean and somewhat 
---
lat with your
jack plane, you want to test it 
---
or “twist.” A board is twisted when two diagonal corners are high and the two others are low — a common problem.
You test 
---
or twist by sighting across “winding sticks” — straight sections o
---

material that are much wider than your board and placed at the near and

---
ar end o
---
 it. When you sight across them, you can see i
---
 the sticks are out
o
---
 alignment because the winding sticks exaggerate the twist. Mark the high
corners (I use chalk) and work the high corners using short, diagonal thrusts

with the jack plane. Then test your results again with the winding sticks.
This procedure takes a little practice, but soon it becomes second nature.

The irons 
---
or the wooden plows are always tapered in their length — they
are quite thick up at the bevel and taper until they are quite thin at the
area where you tap them. This taper works in conjunction with the wooden

wedge o
---

quite ingenious technology.

Irons 
---
or metal plows are not tapered. They are consistent in their thickness 
---
rom the bevel all the way back to the end where they engage the mech-

anism that moves the iron in and out o
---

The bevel o
---
 the irons 
---
or a plow (and most other joinery planes) is usually ground at 30°, which is 5° steeper than the bevel on a bench plane or

block plane. The steeper angle gives you a slightly longer edge li
---
e. And
you'll get even a little more edge li
---
e i
---
you hone your secondary bevel at 35°.

The irons should be ground and honed straight across. No skew. No
curve. The only problem you'll encounter with sharpening a plow iron is i
---


it is too rusted or pitted, especially on its unbeveled 
---
ace. That can produce
a jagged or 
---

Using a Plow Plane
The most important thing about using a plow plane has nothing at all to do
with the tool. Instead, it’s the wood you select 
---
or your project. When you
choose your wood 
---
or the rails and stiles o
---
 a 
---
rame-and-panel construction

you should go to great pains to get the straightest grain possible. Not only
does this look better, but it will be 
---
ar easier 
---
or your plow plane to deal with.

Same goes with your drawer sides and drawer 
---
ronts. Straight-grained
boards make 
---
or better-looking drawer 
---
ronts and drawer sides as well. And
your plow will have a much easier time.
As you've probably realized, plows work (easily) in only one direction
(yes, you can go to strange lengths to avoid this problem). As a result, you

are limited in how you can position the board on the bench so that your tool

works with the grain.
You always want the 
---
ence o
---
 the plane to ride against the board's re
---
erence sur
---
ace, which is 
---
lat, true and in line with the other re
---
erence sur
---
aces

o
---
 your 
---
rame-and-panel construction. This will ensure that all your grooves
line up when you assemble the door.

These constraints can box you ina bit when plowing your grooves. Some-

Two hands; two jobs. Your dominant hand should push

times you will have to plane against the grain. So in order to avoid tearing,
the best strategy is to select boards with dead-straight grain that ideally can
be planed in both directions without problems. Or, at the least, won't tear

out horribly. I
---

---

When you use a plow plane — or any handplane with a 
---

your hands has a distinct job (and there is no cross-training allowed). Your

dominant hand grasps the heel or tote o
---

But wait; there are more di
---

---
erences to learn. Plow planes are not used
like bench planes. Where you start your stroke and where you end it are
di
---

erent. With a bench plane, you start your stroke at the right end and end
on the le
---

---

---

di

You start a 
---

---

---

---

o
---
 the board. Then you start about 6" 
---
rom the end and take a second stroke.
With each stroke you back up a 
---
ew inches until you are making strokes the

---
ull length o
---
 the board.
Why?

The simple answer is that you want to make a clean groove. I
---
 you really

want to know why, read the 
---
ollowing, more complex answer.

When you use a plow with the grain, the cutter will tend to 
---
ollow the
grain direction o
---
 the board. That is the path o
---
 least resistance. The grain

direction might be per
---
ectly straight, but about hal
---
 the time the grain will
push the 
---
ence and tool away 
---
rom the work. This wandering iron can be a
disaster, especially on a long stile. You can ruin the stile with the 
---
irst pass o
---

your plow plane.
So i
---
 you start near the end o
---
 the stile and take a short pass, the iron

will not wander 
---
ar. It can’t. It is traveling only 3". Then when you back up
another 3", that pause gives the iron a chance to correct any small error and
dive deeper. By making the initial groove along the stile in small segments,
you are reducing the chance that you'll make a big error i
---
 you make the 
---
irst

cut with one mighty swoop.

Rabbet planes are so essential to the hand-tool woodworker that it is noth-

ing less than shocking how hard it is to purchase a good one. Yes, there

wooden planes, usually have problems, such as a warped wooden stock, an
iron that needs an overhaul, a ragged-out 
---

---

raised panel.

Skewed or Straight tron?
Rabbet planes are made either with the iron sharpened straight across, or

at about a 20° skew. Should you care? Yes. The skewed iron is harder to

sharpen, but it helps when used with a 
---
ence. The skew pulls the 
---
ence o
---
 the
tool against the work, helping it stay on track. Also, a skew cut makes the

tool easier to push. And the resulting cut across the grain is cleaner than that

with a straight iron.

---

Many beginning woodworkers make the mistake o

Using a Rabbet Plane
There are many ways to use a rabbet plane, and the technique you choose

depends on the tool you have. With a simple rabbet plane, I generally use a

marking gauge or cutting gauge to scribe the width and depth o
---
 the joint on
my piece. Then I tip the tool about 20° and place the tool’s edge (where the
sole meets the sidewall) into my scribe line.

Then I gently push 
---
orward, removing a tiny sliver. A
---
ter a 
---
ew passes
I've created a shoulder line and can begin tipping the body o
---
 the plane toward vertical. I use my 
---
ingers as a 
---
ence but really the shoulder o
---
 the joint

does most o
---
 the guiding.

With the “standing” rabbet planes there is no need to scribe the width
o
---

---
 the

wood — then you need to scribe both.) You can begin this joint with the plane

standing vertical. It’s 
---
airly straight
---
orward. Just as when you use a plow

plane, you should use your dominant hand to push the tool 
---
orward and your
o
---

---
-hand to hold the 
---
ence against the work.

With a moving 
---
illister plane you don’t have to mark any part o
---
 the joint

on your work. Merely set your 
---
ence to the width o
---
 the joint you want. Set
the depth stop to the depth you want. I
---

drop the nicker so it cuts.

I 
---
ind it's a good idea with any 
---

the plane toward you, which makes the nicker scribe the width o
---
 the joint on

yt)

your board. This con

---

Block Plane
You can build 
---
urniture without a block plane. But why should you? The
block plane is one o
---

---
 the Industrial Revolution, in my opinion. With a block plane and a little skill you can accomplish almost any task. These tools trim end grain, 
---
ace grain and whatever
else you ask o
---

---

their lower pitch). They are the most 
---

---
actured. You
can change the pitch o
---
 the tool with great ease and close or open the mouth

with no special tools. And they are simple to set up.

Woodworking purists sco
---

---
 at the tool, but I think that this is only because it doesn’t 
---
it into their narrow tool list. I
---

spouting o

In

I did not sharpen it. It cut the pine surprisingly well. I can remember being

Low Angle or Standard?
Block planes come in two 
---
lavors: low-angle or standard-angle. Low-angle
tools have the iron bedded on a ramp that is 12° o
---

---
 o
---
 the sole. Standard
planes have a 20° bed. Low-angle planes make it easier to achieve lower

ESSENTIAL

PLANES

101

planing angles, which are nice 
---
or end grain. Standard-angle planes make it

easier to achieve higher planing angles, which are nice 
---
or reducing tear-out.
The reason | always use a low-angle block plane is two-
---
old.
1. The lower angle makes 
---
or a more compact tool that 
---
its better in my
hand. Your mileage may vary here.

2. With the low-angle plane you have a wider variety o
---
 planing angles

available to you. You can achieve angles as low as 37°. Standard-angle
planes can only go as low as 45°, i
---
 you want the edge to last more than a 
---
ew
strokes. Both planes can achieve high-planing angles. So the low-angle tools

are more versatile.
So I see no reason to even own a standard-angle block plane. And I don't.

Adjustable Mouth or Not?
Low-rent block planes generally have a 
---
ixed mouth, though there are some
nice small block planes with 
---
ixed mouths. I pre
---
er an adjustable mouth.
Why? When I am using a block plane to true end grain, I don't want the
leading corner o
---
 the work diving into the mouth aperture. When I work in
tricky grain, I will use every weapon available to me to attempt to reduce
tearing — including an adjustable mouth.

And when I need to hog o
---

---
 material, I simply open the mouth as wide as
it will go. Easy. I
---
 you have only one block plane, I recommend a low-angle
tool with an adjustable mouth.

Lever Cap, Knuckle-joint Cap or Screw Cap?
There are several ways to secure the iron in a block plane. One system uses
some sort o
---
 screw mechanism that you turn to tighten the plane's cap against

the iron. The screw can be above or below the plane’s cap.
I want to be somewhat speci
---
ic here, so please bear with me. Almost ev-

ery block plane has a screw in the center o
---
 the bed that is the pivot point o
---


everything that holds the iron in place. I
---
 the lever cap is also tensioned by a
screwing mechanism, then that’s the part that I’m talking about. It is usually

a large screw i
---

---

systems work 
---
ine.

I
---

---

center o

---

i

other ways work

ine, too

thing. It is quick, but the downside is that you can only control the 
---
inal ten-

sion on the blade by adjusting the screw in the center o
---
 the bed.
I
---
 the lever cap actually has a lever (how novel!), then the whole iron is

locked in place with a cam action that is set in motion with the lever on the
cap. Press the lever one way and the cap locks the plane down. Press it the
other way and the cap is loose and can be removed. This relationship between the cap and the iron is regulated again by the screw in the center. Get

it set right and the cap will lock and unlock like clockwork.
Personally I pre
---

---
eed. They

How to Use a Block Plane
Unlike other traditional tools, pretty much anything goes when it comes to

using a block plane. You can push it, pull it, hold it with one hand or two,

work edges or 
---
aces or ends — even clamp it upside down in a vise and push
the parts across the sole.

Just keep the mouth tight to reduce tearing — but not so tight that it clogs.
Because the tools are called on to trim end grain, many woodworkers

discover a rude surprise when they plane end grain

Router Plane
Router planes need a new name. Because so many woodworkers associate
the word “router” with the electric tool, I think it tarnishes the reputation o
---


this workhorse. The word “router” has its root in the verb “to root” — like a
pig roots 
---
or tru
---

---
les.
Router planes do just that. They root beneath the sur
---
ace o
---
 a board
and remove material with great accuracy. They can root around in the bottom o
---
 a dado, groove or rabbet to make it per
---
ectly true. They can root on
the cheeks o
---
 your tenons to make them true, centered on your stock and a
consistent thickness. They can root out the bottom o
---
 your hinge mortises so
they are all the same depth, which prevents an ill-
---
itting door.

But what they don’t do are the tasks associated with the electric router.
They don’t make moulding. They don’t cut deep mortises. They don’t per-


---
orm all manner o
---
 trickery when upside down in a table.
Tam one o
---
 the biggest 
---
ans o
---
 router planes. In 
---
act, I gave up my electric
router 
---
or this book. But I’d never give up my router planes.

Wood, Iron or Something Else?
Early router planes were usually shop-made tools. You would take a piece o
---

scrap bannister rail and cut a mortise in it. Then you would shape a worn-out


---
ile and 
---
it it in the mortise and tighten things up with a wedge. The cutter
typically did not bend 90° like those on the modern router planes. Instead, it
came straight down and would do more scraping than planing.
Later wooden routers, which are still made today, have an “L”-shaped
cutter and a simple blade-adjustment mechanism — usually a thumbscrew.

This was the style I used 
---
or years and it works well.
The downside to the wooden routers is that they are usually quite big.

The wood is thick and the sole is wide. So they are 
---
ine when you are work-

The iron router planes are thinner and smaller, so they can go more plac-

Adjusting the Depth
The depth o
---

Using a Router Plane
Router planes are rarely used by themselves to create a joint. Usually they

are the great precision janitors that clean up the mess le
---
t by the other tools.

You might cut a tenon cheek with a saw, but it is the router plane that ensures it is 
---
lat. You might saw out the walls o
---
 your dado and remove

the

gross waste with a chisel, but it is the router plane that ensures the dado is

---
lat and the same depth as all your other dados.

Like any plane, routers can take a 
---
airly heavy cut when used across the
grain, such as when you remove the waste in a dado. Typically, however, you
will want to set the router plane to take a light cut. This makes the tool easier
to handle and generally improves the sur
---
ace 
---
inish le
---
t behind on the wood.

Final Notes on Planes
Learning to use a handplane is like learning to ride a bicycle. There is a steep
learning curve at 
---
irst. You must learn to sharpen the tool, set it properly and

move it across the material in a way that gives you smooth sur
---
aces instead
o
---
 chewed ones.

With bicycles, you have to learn to balance, steer and pedal 
---
orward, all
at the same time. When I taught my oldest child how to ride a bike, we had
many evenings that ended with a tremendous wreck in the school parking
lot, and skinned arms and legs.

Then one evening, it clicked and she could ride a bike. And she'll now be
able to do it 
---
or the rest o
---
 her li
---
e.

The same thing happens with handplanes. You will struggle. You will

ESSENTIAL

PLANES

113

have short bursts o
---
 success 
---
ollowed by spectacular 
---
ailures, which is why
it is good to practice on scrap wood. But one day the planets will align and
everything will work. And at that moment you will also have mastered all the

other planes — they all work using the same principles.
Don’t give up — not a
---
ter the 
---
irst day, week or month.

---
 you are a machinist or engineer, this chapter might cause you to re-

---
ree speech. I don’t deal well with
digital readouts. I rarely measure anything less than 1/16". And I pre
---

---
rom wood when practical.
Why wood? Well, it is lighter in weight so that my measuring tools can
be longer. It is less expensive. And it can easily be trued with my hand tools.
Years ago our shop spent more than $100 on a precision metal straight-

edge 
---
rom Starrett. During the course o
---

---
irst year it was dropped and

ul 
---

I keep it in our shop at work to remind mysel

buying this expensive error-detecting mechanism.

But not every measuring tool can or should be made o
---

you run a kni
---
e along will last longer i
---
 they are metallic. And some metal
tools are less likely to wear at critical points and there
---
ore last longer. It takes
a little common sense.
So what about the question o
---
 accuracy? Why shouldn't we use the best
digital technology in woodworking? There is nothing inherently wrong with

digital tools. They might make your results more precise, but they don't en-

sure accuracy. In other words, you might end up with a board that is precisely .6275" thick, but it still might be bowed or twisted, and ruin an assembly.

There also is a deeper issue here that is important to me. We do not experience 
---
inished 
---
urniture with calipers. Instead we experience it with our

eyes, our 
---
ingers and the rest o
---
 our body that comes in contact with it.
All your lumber might be exactly 3/4" thick, but i
---
 the resulting project
looks like crap or is uncom
---
ortable, then it is a 
---
ailure.
There are two important boatbuilding expression that I like to use when
discussing measuring.
e “I
---
 it looks 
---
air, it is 
---
air.” In other words, our eyes are better than our
measuring instruments.

kitchen cabinet to be 13" deep. Let's begin by exploring the most important

Gauges
In handwork, gauges are the most important marking tool. You should start

with one, but you might end up with several more in your chest, including
ones that you have made, because it is handy to have several gauges set up to

dimensions you will return to many times during a project.

I can work with one gauge, but I 
---
ind that two gauges are better. And
three is almost enough.

Gauges are the tools that guide other tools. They lay out the lines that
you work to: the baseline o
---
 where your dovetails take wing 
---
rom your board.

The width and depth o
---
 a rabbet. The cheek o
---
 a tenon. The wall o
---
 a mortise.
There are so many types o
---

types and combinations. But they do have some common 
---
eatures that will
help you

select a good one.

Cutting or Marking?
The two major 
---
amilies o
---
 gauges are the cutting gauges and the marking

gauges. The cutting gauge uses a kni
---
e to make the line. The marking gauge

uses a conical pin. Both need to be sharpened and both 
---
orms should cut the
wood — not just dent it.

Cutting gauges do a better job when working across the grain. The thin
kni
---
e edge slices the 
---
ibers, even when the kni
---
e becomes abit dull. The result

is that you can mark dovetail baselines and the like 
---
or a long time between
sharpenings. When working parallel to the grain, cutting gauges occasion-

ally will 
---
ollow the grain o
---
 the wood. This can occur with any gauge, and it
is mostly a problem when you are dealing with a wood that has a signi
---
icant
di
---

---
erence in its density between its earlywood and latewood.

a cutting gauge.

Do You Need a Mortise Gauge?
Many tool inventories list a mortise gauge, which is a gauge with two pins
so that you can mark out both walls o
---
 a mortise or both cheeks o
---
 a tenon

simultaneously. I have one, but I don’t use it as much as I thought I would.
When I mark out mortises, I use a simple cutting gauge and de
---
ine only one

wall o
---
 the mortise. The mortise chisel itsel
---
 de
---
ines the other wall o
---
 the
mortise.
When marking tenon cheeks, I typically use two settings (or two gauges)

to mark the cheeks. Yeah, a mortise gauge would be more convenient I suppose, but I never 
---
eel compelled to use one when marking tenons. Also, i
---

you use machines to dimension your stock, I think a mortise gauge is a waste
o
---

---
our-squared stock allows you to use one setting o
---

---

---
aces o
---

your boards are parallel, thanks to the machines.

So I’m not going to tell you to buy a mortise gauge. But I’m also not going to tell you to 
---
orsake one. Try one out in the store — they can be complex

buggers. Some people like that trait. Others are turned o

Usng a Gauge
The most important thing about using a gauge is to pay attention to the

cutter itsel

---

The key to all hand tools is to know how to sharpen them and start them
in the wood. Gauges are no exception. The kni

---

With a marking gauge you have a pin that has one

---

---

---
lat part o
---

---

---

easily through the wood.

No matter which type o

---

usea

Panel Gauge

ix this. Get some wood bleach, the oxalic acid type. Mix it up and apply
the bleach with a gray pad and don’t 
---
orget the nitrile gloves. This process
usually lightens the wood but leaves the ink markings intact. I’ve made rules
look as good as new with this technique. A
---
ter a 
---
ew minutes, wash the acid

o

---
 with water. When the rule is dry, protect your tool with wax.

Winding Sticks
Winding sticks are like the spirit levels o
---
 the carpentry world. They tell you
whether your work is 
---
lat and when it is in twist. They are no more than
two long sticks that have parallel long edges. No more. They don't have to

be 
---
ancy with ivory and ebony inlays. They don’t have to be the same width.
They don’t have to be di
---

---
erent colors. Just long and parallel.
I like winding sticks because they trade in hyperbole. Like a Pat Conroy
novel, there is great truth in great exaggeration. Winding sticks are always

longer than the width o
---
 your work, so they exaggerate any twist in your
board. The longer the winding sticks, the more they exaggerate. The more
they exaggerate, the more accurate they are.

I have a pair o

Whenever you make a shop tool, choose a hard-wearing quartersawn wood.

I like hard maple or even some exotics when I make winding sticks. Make

is diagonal to that scrawl mark. Boards twist, which means they corkscrew.

two will be low.

56" Wooden Straightedge
No matter what I wrote about aluminum winding sticks above, ignore it.

You should make your own 36"-long wooden straightedge. You should make
it completely by hand to prove to yoursel
---
 that you can true a board to an
insane level o
---
 accuracy. And you should keep that stick as a reminder o
---


what is possible with hand tools.
When it comes to making a wooden straightedge, there is little agreement
on how to do it. And i
---
 a bunch o
---
 crusty 
---
arts can’t agree on how to make a
simple 
---
ricking stick, then I think you know why people keep writing new

woodworking books, year a
---
ter year.
I have my own way o
---
 making my stick. And I have my reasons. Here
are the details on my 36" rod o
---
 per
---
ection. I like my straightedge to be 1/2"
thick so that it is easy to balance on edge on my work, yet easy to see light

under it. Thinner straightedges tend to topple. Thicker sticks make it harder
to see gaps under them.

I like a stick that is 2-1/2" wide. That width is easy to grasp, yet wide

LOREEN

enough

that

GleeMEASURING

TO.oO L's

ior9

you

won't mistake it 
---
or
a cuto
---

---
 
---
rom a typi-

cal rip and throw it
away.
I

make

my

straightedges so they
have

only

one

true

edge. The other edge
tapers 
---
rom the cen-

ter to the ends. In other words, my straightedge is 2-1/2"-wide in the middle
and 1-1/4"-wide at its ends. Why? Well the theory behind this shape is two
---
old. One: You only have to keep one edge true, and because o
---
 the taper you
will never con
---
use the true edge with the other edge. I'll buy that 
---
or a dollar.
Second, as wood acclimates to its environment, most o
---
 the moisture
passes in and out o
---
 the end grain o
---
 the board. The 
---
ace grain isn’t as involved in moisture exchange. This is important.

A wooden smoothing plane

is co
---

---
in-shaped to expose as much end grain as possible to allow the plane's
body to react quickly to changes in humidity. This makes the tool more predictable.
With a wooden

straightedge, the two tapers expose a considerable

amount o
---
 end grain. This allows the middle o
---
 the straightedge to expand
and contract with changes in environment much 
---
aster than i
---
 the straightedge resembled a metal machinist’s straightedge with two parallel edges.

With that shape, the ends would expand and contract 
---
aster than the middle.
That's the theory. Does this shape really work in practice? I think so. My
straightedge has remained true 
---
or insanely long periods o
---
 time. In 
---
act, I’ve
trued it only once since I made this version about six years ago. O
---
 course,

I wasn’t a dolt about making this straightedge. I used some dense exotic
redheart that had been kicking around our shop 
---
or years. It’s heavy stu
---

---
.

How to Make a Good Stratghtedge
Pick the best, most stable and dense wood you can 
---
ind. Wood selection is
everything. Dress the board as accurately as possible. Keep working the
stock until it is truly 
---
lat and straight. I
---
 the board resists your e
---

---
orts to true
it, then put it aside 
---
or some other project such as a boomerang or a weenie
roast. Pick your wood like you pick a spouse. Good looks are great. But you
want something that is agreeable and stable in the long-term.
Once you have a 1/2"-thick stick that is dressed to 2-1/2" wide and 36"
long, draw the tapers on one 
---
ace and saw or plane those tapers to shape. |
leave an area o
---
 about 5" long that is the 
---
ull 2-1/2" width. Then I taper each
edge to 1-1/4". A
---
ter you cut those tapers, plane some small cham
---
ers on the
tapered edges. These make the straightedge nice to hold and make it less
likely that the thing will end up in the scrap bin.

And 
---
inally, true the long edge that will be your re
---
erence sur
---
ace. Take
your time. Ensure it is 90° to both 
---
aces o
---
 your stick. Then ensure the long

edge is true. There are complex machinist-style ways to determine this using

two other straight sur
---
aces, but here is the woodworker’s down-and-dirty
in-the-
---
ield way.
Find a piece o
---
 posterboard or plywood, or a big sheet o
---
 paper. Place

the straightedge 
---
lat on this sur
---
ace and trace along its re
---
erence edge with a
pencil. Flip the straightedge 180° and compare the re
---
erence edge with your
pencil line. I
---
 there are any gaps between the line and your straightedge, you
have work to do to correct things.

Other Stick Shapes
There are other old sources that will tell you to make your straightedge ina

di
---

---
erent shape. Here is a brie
---
 discussion o
---
 them.

MARKING

&MEASURING

TOOLS

141

* Parallel long edges. Consistent thickness. Consistent width.

This sort o
---
 straightedge might be 
---
ine. Or the ends might swell more
quickly than the middle when the wet season comes to your little corner o
---

the world. Likewise, the ends might dry out 
---
aster when the humidity drops.
Or everything will be 
---
ine and your straightedge will be as consistently right

as New Hampshire.
¢ Parallel long edges. Consistent width. The thickness tapers down to
1/4" at the edges.

This is an English variant I 
---
ound in an early 20th-century text, and it
resembles some machinist straightedges I've seen. The theory is that the thin

1/4" pro
---
ile o
---
 the edges will make it easy 
---
or you to see errors as you drag
the straightedge across your questionable boards.
I don’t disagree with that statement. The thin pro
---
ile will indeed per
---
orm
as advertised. Where this shape 
---
alls down is when you are checking a long
edge o
---
 a board to see i
---
 it is true. Typically you balance a straightedge on

a questionable edge to test its truth. I
---
 the straightedge spins on its middle,
then you have a board with a hump in the middle o
---
 the edge that needs to
be removed.

I
---
 the straightedge drags at the ends, then you either have a

dead-straight edge or a hollow (called a concavity) along the edge. Hollows

are always pre
---
erable to humps in my book.
I
---
 you have a straightedge with a thin edge, it won't easily balance on

your edge. In 
---
act, it is 
---
airly useless 
---
or checking edges without an eagle eye
to spot gaps between the straightedge and the board in question.

* Consistent thickness. One straight edge. The other edge curves in a

broad arc.
This traditional shape works well, too. It just looks butt-ugly to me, like
a pastry tool or something used in the oyster industry. You can make this

shape and it will work 
---
ine, but I think that getting a consistent curve over
36" is harder than making a couple straight tapers.
Bottom line: Make one. No matter which shape appeals to you, making

How to Make a Good Square
A
---
ter you make a straightedge and maybe a couple winding sticks, a square
is an excellent project because it has only one joint to 
---
uss with. Make your
stock 
---
rom 3/4" x 1-5/8" x 10" material and your blade 
---
rom 1/4" x 2-1/2" x

LSECos = L OlO Ea Car ease
TI HeeA NUAIR

144

15" material that is slightly over-thick at the start. Quartersawn stock is best.
Saw a 1/4"'-wide x 2"-deep notch in the handle and true the 
---
loor o
---
 the
notch with a 1/4"-wide chisel. Then plane the blade until it slips into the
notch without bending the two 
---
orks o
---
 your notch. Cut any decorative de-

tails you desire on the ends o
---

together. Use a slow-setting glue such as liquid hide glue or white PVA glue
so you can care
---
ully manipulate the blade and stock so the inside corner o
---


the square is dead-nuts on 90°. Don’t 
---
uss over the angle on the outside o
---

the square. That can easily be trued a

inside angle is a bear to 
---
ix a
---

A

ameter dowels. True the outside o
---

---

---

---

blade. Flip the square 90° and press the stock against the same edge. Compare the blade to the line you scribed. I
---

up to its name. Otherwise, you have work to do. A
---
ter you square the outside

o
---

This square is one o

---

---
or a budding anarchist.

Sliding Bevel Square
By this point I’m certain you are expecting me to wax on (perhaps to later
wax o
---

---
) poetic about wooden shop-made sliding bevels. Wrong. I own one.
I hate it. My mom collects wooden measuring tools, and all the wooden slid-

ing bevels I’ve seen at her house suck eggs. In 
---
act, most o
---
 the metal ones
suck eggs in equal measure. Precious 
---
ew bevel squares are worth owning.

Why? They don’t lock down the blade worth a damn. Or, i
---
 they do lock
well, then the locking mechanism almost always gets in the way o
---
 your us-

MARKING
& MEASURING

TOOLS

146

ing the square in a normal-like 
---
ashion.

As a result, whenever I use a sliding bevel, I end up babying the thing
and spending way too much e
---

---
ort trying to ensure that nothing bumps it
even slightly. And i
---
 something does give my sliding bevel a love tap, then

I'm going to check the square’s accuracy again.
This is a big waste o
---
 shop time during a project.
So what makes a good square? It’s easier to talk about what makes a bad

square. All the squares that lock right at the joint where the blade pivots
in the handle are sub-optimal in my experience. I
---
 the square locks with a

rotating lever, then it’s inevitable that the lever will inter
---
ere with the square
when you try to press the stock or blade against your work. I
---
 it locks with
a thumbscrew at the pivot point, youll never be able to get it tight enough.

Yes, I tried adding lock washers — everything but J.B. Weld glue. Other
sliding bevels lock with a cam lever at the pivot point. These can be almost

Better in metal. Sliding bevels are tricky tools. Even expensive ones are poor. You need
one that locks securely and doesn’t inter
---
ere with layout. In general, the ones that lock at
the base o
---
 the handle are the best.

146

THE

DS
ANARCHIS

TOO

Grrr sr

acceptable. You will have to tighten up the slack in the mechanism, which is
usually controlled with a screw, to the point where the sliding bevel is a real
e
---

---
ort to lock. But when it locks, it locks reasonably tight.
So what works? I’ve had much better results with sliding bevels that lock
at the base o
---
 the handle, either with a knob or thumbscrew. These sliding
bevels — sometimes called butt-locking bevels — have an internal mechanism
that wedges the blade in position (instead o
---
 merely bending the handle to

squeeze the blade at the pivot point).

The Important Points
The most important parts are the points o
---
 the dividers. They should be needle sharp and close together so they are touching or almost touching. When

I salvage old dividers, most o
---

ort is on 
---

bite into the wood with very little downward pressure but leave only a pinprick behind. Blunt dividers aren't accurate. They make large depressions in
your work, which makes it impossible to 
---

---

---

---

A Full Range o
---
Movement
Many dividers use spring action to maintain their setting. The circular spring
at the top o
---
 the legs o
---
the dividers tries to push the points apart. A machine

screw and the tool's adjuster nut restrain the spring and keep the points a
consistent distance apart. With some old or cheap dividers, the spring is

weak. As a result, you will open the tool up to a certain point and it will become 
---
laccid and useless. When you are trying out a pair o
---
 dividers in the

store, see how wide you can open them be
---
ore you buy them. You should be
able to open them up to the 
---
ull width allowed by the machine screw.

More Friction, Please
Old-school dividers didn’t use a spring. They used 
---
riction to keep the points
in position. With these sorts o
---
 dividers, the two legs are typically hinged together with a series o
---
 leaves, like a 
---
inger joint. Then the hinge pin through

the leaves is peened to tighten up the action. So the 
---
riction is a good thing.
I know idiots who have oiled up their old dividers and worked them to the
point where the legs move easily — too easily — and the dividers will lose their
setting i
---
 you just breathe on them wrong.
You want your dividers to be di
---

---
icult to adjust. In 
---
act, I have several

pair that had seen a lot o
---
 use and were likely worn loose. Rust actually
saved the day, making them di
---

---
icult to move again. Weird world, huh? I
---

your dividers are too loose, you can peen the hinge pin to tighten things up.
Just set the tool 
---
lat on a steel plate and strike the hinge pin with a nailset

and hammer. Keep tapping and testing until you get some pleasing 
---
riction.

---

---

Tang, Socket or Japanese?
There are three basic styles o
---
 chisels to choose 
---
rom: tang, socket or a Japanese variant that combines the two. Each has advantages and disadvantages.
While other writers might disagree with my 
---
ollowing assessment, I am ignoring them.
Tang chisels are the simplest 
---
orm and the easiest to repair when something goes wrong. In a tang chisel, the metal is the male part o
---
 the way the
handle and steel connect, and the wood is the 
---
emale. The tang is usually a
rectangular bit o
---
 steel (sometimes rough or barbed) that is driven into a
stepped hole that is bored in the handle.
Tang chisels can be simply that: a piece o
---
 barbed steel and a wooden
handle. But this simple 
---
orm can be 
---
ragile. The tang o
---
 the chisel is like a
wedge in some 
---
orms. Beat the handle hard enough and the tang will split
the handle. To prevent (or slow) this splitting action, some tang chisels have

a 
---
errule, which is a circular band o
---
 metal that encompasses the point where
the steel and wood meet. The 
---
errule helps to prevent the wood 
---
rom splitting at the point where the wood is the thinnest and the steel is the thickest.
Other chisels don’t have a 
---
errule, but instead have a 
---
lat land where the

wood and steel meet. This kind o
---
 joint is harder 
---
or the manu
---
acturer to 
---
it
and in my experience it is also less durable. You'll see this kind o
---
 connection
on some traditional mortise chisels. It works, up to a point. Then the handle

splits. I know this 
---
rom splitting many o
---
 them.
Some tools also have a leather washer between the steel and the wood.

This is supposed to cushion the blows and reduce the chance o
---
 the wood
splitting. Does it work? Can’t really say. It seems to help.
The second type o
---
 chisel is the socket construction. In this sort o
---
 chisel

the steel is the 
---
emale part o
---
 the joint — it is 
---
ormed into a round and tapered
socket. This is a more costly way o
---
 making a chisel. And the wood is the

male part —it is a matching tapered piece o
---
 wood that is driven into the steel.
The downside to this arrangement is that it is a more di
---

---
icult connection

Why Western Chisels Might be Better (
---
or Westerners)
Japanese chisels are amazing. Really. While there are some real bright spots
among those who make Western-style chisels, overall the Japanese still have
everyone else beat. But 
---
or Western-style work, I recommend you buy Western chisels. Why? The metric system.
Japanese chisels are handmade, and are almost always made with metric
widths. In many cases, the wood doesn’t give a hoot that your chisel is metric. You can still pare and chop out a lot o
---
 waste without worrying about
millimeters or 
---
ractional inches.
But things change when you become a hard-core hand-tool user. You
need to make a decision as to whether you run a metric shop or an Imperial
shop. That's because it is ideal to have your tools agree. I
---
 you plow 1/4"
grooves with your plow plane, it's handy to have 1/4"-wide mortise chisels,
and not some nearby metric equivalent.
I end up using my chisels as measuring instruments. When I need to
lay out 1/4"-wide tenon shoulders on a rail, I'll o
---
ten turn to my 1/4" chisel

instead o
---
 a rule. The chisel is 
---
ar more accurate and is also a marking tool as
well. But i
---
 | have metric chisels, I’m a bit hamstrung and need to rely on the
less-accurate rule or combination square.

I'm not saying you can’t make metric chisels work in an Imperial shop.
You can work around almost any constraint i
---
 you really put your mind to it.
But i
---
 you want to reduce the complexity o
---
 the cra
---
t, it only makes sense to
have all your tools tuned to the same

measurement

system.

Mortise chisels abide by many o

Here’s the bee
---

Hi

snap) and handles (I've split many).

It should go without saying that
mortise chisels have to take a beating. The handle should be stout and made


---
rom a wood

that doesn’t split

---
you have seen

and the tool’s metal socket or tang is mushroomed and split.

Some woodworkers contend that the way to prevent the handle 
---

---
rom a wood that is so
---
ter than the

chisel. That way the mallet absorbs the damage.

162

My experience is mixed on this point. I've destroyed a 
---
ew beech handles
with lignum vitae mallets, which would seem to support the theory. But I've
cracked just as many beech handles with beech mallets. And so
---
ter mallets,
such as one I have that’s made o
---
 so
---
t maple, just won't deliver the punch
needed to make the steel penetrate the wood. It 
---
eels like hitting a brick o
---


Jell-O with a marshmallow. So there’s no point to using it.
I have an opinion on the topic o
---
 chisel handles, but I reserve the right to
change it. I think that the wood needs to be consistent in its texture. In other
words, open-grained woods such as oak and ash are no good. They have ar-

eas that are dense and areas that are not. As a result, these woods tend to be

---
ragile when struck repeatedly. Closed-grain woods that are heavy and resist
splitting are ideal. Think beech, black locust and hornbeam. I'm sure there
are other species like this, especially exotics.
But picking one o
---
 these woods isn’t enough. The grain has to be dead
straight 
---
rom the striking sur
---
ace to the bolster or 
---
errule. I
---
 the grain runs

through the handle at an angle other than 90°, the handle is compromised.
This is not theory. This is experience.
There are other things you can do to help reduce splitting. Covering your

mallet with 1/4" shoe leather helps. Adding a leather striking pad to the chisel
handle seems to help. Adding an iron ring around the striking end o
---
 the
chisel de
---
initely helps. And keeping your tools sharp helps, too.

urniture.
Those are the sizes o

Then buy other mortise chisel sizes as you need them. I
---

---

smaller tools. Don’t 
---
all

Handle Shapes
With mortise chisels you can get round or oval in cross section. The oval
handles, typically 
---
ound on English tang-style mortisers, are superior be-

cause the long axis o
---

---

---
ront
edge o
---
 the tool. You pick it up and you instantly know where the edge is and

unconsciously rotate the handle to the right position.
Round handles work 
---
ine, but you'll always be eyeballing the steel to
ensure you are driving the chisel into the right place.

Don't Forget the Metal Bit
With all my chatter about the wooden part o
---

---

---

---
or a mortise chisel. For centuries they were made

dered metals. I’ve used all these metals and can tell you that every steel is a

---

---

The shape o

---
ine. Other
mortise chisels, usually the English ones that look like a dagger, are trapezoidal in cross-section. They look like a pyramid with the top pointy bit sheared
o
---

---
. In chisel language, the sides o
---

---

and on these chisels the 
---

Why? Well it seems to retard sticking. In other words, when you drive
your chisel deep into the wood, tapered 
---
lanks help the chisel release. Aggressive mortising can result in your tool getting wedged into the work i
---
 you
go all Conan the Barbarian on the handle.
Which blade shape should you choose? Either is 
---
ine. Sticking mortise
chisels aren't the scourge o
---

---
t. They happen, but you can wiggle the

thing out. So tapered 
---
lanks are a convenience, not a category killer.

Bevel-down or Bevel-up?
Oh, you thought we were 
---
inished with this topic when you 
---
inished the
section on handplanes? No such luck. Like planes, spokeshaves come with
these two kinds o
---
 bodies. Most metal spokeshaves are bevel-down tools and
most wooden-bodied tools are bevel-up tools. Yes, Poindexter, I know there
are some notable exceptions.
The bevel-down tools are most similar to handplanes. The tool's iron is
secured on a bed that is pitched at about 45°. No matter what angle you

sharpen on the tip o
---
 the iron, it will always cut at 45°, which is a good
workaday angle 
---
or hardwoods and so
---
twoods.
The bevel-up tools are wilder. The iron o
---
 the tool is also its sole. This
unusual detail can create some interesting cutting situations. You can end up

with a tool with a crazy-low cutting angle — somewhere slightly north o
---
 25°.
A low-angle block plane typically has a cutting angle o
---
 37°.
That low angle means the tool can slice through woods with little e
---

---
ort,
especially end grain, and leave a beauti
---
ul 
---
inish. The downside to the low

angle is that you are going to su
---

---
er more tear-out when you hit a patch o
---

reversing grain.

I
---
 your brain is ticking, then it probably just occurred to you to sharpen

a higher angle on the bevel-up spokeshave and create a high-angle spokeshave. Well, maybe. I’ve put some steeper pitches (such as 50°) on my bevelup spokeshaves with inconsistent results. The steeper pitch seems to increase

clogging in my experience.

So what do you do? Well I 
---
ind that the bevel-up shave is great when
working with green lumber, a common task when making chairs. Tear-out
is not nearly the same problem with wet wood as it is in dry stu
---

---
. So the

---
 the tool allows you to shape green wood quickly and with little
e

---

---

clear choice.

But i
---

Now that I’ve tried to sell you on having a shave with an adjuster, let me

metal, bevel-down shaves are bulletproo

The bevel-up shaves are a di

mechanisms. One gets messed up easily; the other is a robust pain in the rear.

---

---

The other common way to adjust bevel-up shaves is quite robust. It looks

---

raise and lower the jackscrews then reassemble the tool and test your set-up.
Disassemble and tweak again until you reach the desired result.
On the one hand, once you get the jackscrews in the right place, the tool
is pretty well set. The downside is that the set-up process is arduous and

making adjustments on the 
---
ly ain’t gonna happen.

The source o

---

Card Scrapers
I've always disliked the term “card scraper.” Yeah, the tool looks like a card;
I’m OK with that part. It’s the word “scraper” that bothers me. It gives the
impression that the tool is used 
---
or coarse work, such as removing paint, 
---
in-

ish or soap scum 
---
rom your bathtub.

The truth is that the card scraper is probably the most delicate and sensitive tool in my chest and is able to remove tear-out on boards that my planes
can’t tame. But I guess no one would buy the tool i
---
 it were called the “little

desert 
---
lower wispy maker.” So “card scraper” it is.
Card scrapers have always been made 
---

Vintage card scrapers are uncommon. I assume that is because these were
used up or thrown away by some anal-retentive relative who thought they

were just junk the woodworker had hoarded (imagine that). I
---

ore being taper-ground. The di
---

---
erence between the thinnest

and thickest scrapers is immense, and it makes a signi
---
icant di
---

---
erence in
how they work.

Thin scrapers are easy to wield. They bend to the correct curvature with
little thumb pressure to remove the smallest bit o
---
 tear-out in a localized area.

The downside is that they cannot make a sur
---
ace 
---
latter. In 
---
act, the thinnest

scrapers will make a sur
---
ace look more rippled by removing only the so
---
t
earlywood in some species. That is how sensitive these suckers are.

So thin scrapers shouldn't be used on a sur
---
ace that’s 
---
resh 
---
rom a saw
(band saw, handsaw, whatever saw). The thin scraper will 
---
ollow or skip

over the low points to create a sur
---
ace that’s just as rippled and twice as ugly.
However, when you use these thin scrapers on a previously 
---
lattened
sur
---
ace and are only seeking to remove a little bit o
---

really shine. Plus, your thumbs will thank you. Thin scrapers can be used 
---
or
much longer than their thicker cousins.
Thick scrapers are a di
---

---
erent animal. Use them 
---
or a 
---
ew minutes, and it

will 
---
eel like your thumbs are going to catch 
---
ire and break o
---

---
 (and perhaps

not in that order). It can be really hard to bend them to the correct curvature


---
or the work at hand. Some toolmakers sell scraper holders that do the bending 
---
or you. I do not like these devices. Good scraping technique requires

you to vary the pitch and curvature o
---
 the tool with every stroke. Scraper
holders prevent you 
---
rom varying the curvature, and this makes it hard to

---
ind the sweet spot with the tool.
So what does all this mean? Choose a thin scraper when you are dealing
with sur
---
aces that are 
---
lat and nearly 
---
inished. Choose thick scrapers when

you are trying to do some smoothing as you clean things up. A good thin
scraper is in the .02"-thick neighborhood. Thick scrapers are just north o
---

03" thick. The tools at the extreme margins o

Hard or So
---
t?
When it comes to the Rockwell hardness o
---
 a scraper, I tend to err a bit on
the so
---
t side. Hard scrapers — more than 51 on the Rockwell “C” hardness

---
rom the package 
---

---

---

---
ia. And turning a hook

books, because the scraper will just mock you. With hard scrapers you need

to come loaded 
---
or bear. Carbide. Polished.
However, i
---
 your scraper is vintage or sharpens up more easily than you

expected, you should be wary. Too-so
---
t scrapers are worse than too-hard
scrapers. Why? Because they don’t get any work done. It’s shavings, shav-

ings, then dust, dust. In other words, it’s a lot more trouble than it is worth
because you'll 
---
eel like you are always sharpening.

Driving chisels is serious business. The wrong mallet will wear you out, miss
the mark or split a chisel handle. In

one chisel mallet, especially i

Round Mallets
Mallets that are turned typically have heads that taper (the taper 
---
ollows

the same principle used in designing the square-head mallets) plus round

handles. They are pre
---
erred by woodworkers who also carve — the roundhead mallet is 
---
airly standard carving equipment. Some woodworkers report
that the round mallet provides more control when driving edge tools, and |

tend to agree.
I became a 
---
an o
---
 round mallets when I started using one with an indestructible head — it’s maple impregnated with resin. There also are roundheaded mallets made 
---
rom brass, urethane and other tough materials. Why?
Because many round wooden mallets tend to get chewed up pretty quickly

because you are beating both the 
---
ace grain and edge grain o
---

mallet as you work. The 
---
ace grain is not as durable as the edge grain.
I
---
 you have a lathe and can spin out a new mallet in a 
---
ew minutes, then

the low durability isn’t much o
---
 a 
---
actor. No lathe? You'll get grumpy pretty
quickly.

Wooden mallets with round heads tend to be made 
---
rom woods that can
take a beating, such as lignum vitae or black locust. These tend to hold up
pretty well.

Braves or Steel Mallets
Other chisel mallets look more like hammers — they have brass or steel
heads. These are pre
---
erred by woodworkers who drive Japanese chisels,
which have a 
---
errous hoop around the handle.

The handle’s hoop allows the handle to take some serious punishment
without splitting. I have never split the handle o

The big advantage o

The Hammers Head
Most woodworkers like a claw hammer with a head that weighs between

13 oz. and 16 oz. The overall length o
---

hammers don’t have the punch you need, and longer ones are hard to aim.

or dismantling

About Vintage Hammers
Most modern hammers have an “adze-eye” head to help keep the head a
---
-


---
ixed to the handle. The adze-eye head has an hourglass-shaped hole through
the steel head anda long neck. So when you a
---

---
ix the handle and wedge it in

the top o
---
 the head's eye, the head is much more likely to stay in place.

Early hammers might not have an adze-eye head, which was an innovation credited to blacksmith David Maydole, o
---
 Norwich, N.Y. He 
---
irst
marketed his adze-eye hammer in 1840. He never patented it and it quickly

became the industry standard.
Pre-1840 hammers use a variety o
---
 methods to keep the head attached
to the handle. The most common

method that survives today is to strap the

head to the handle. So-called “strapped” hammers have two iron or steel
“ears” that extend 
---
rom the hammer’s head down the handle a 
---
ew inches.
The straps are riveted through the handle, which generally does a good job
o
---
 keeping the head and handle married. However, it does make the hammer
a little nose-heavy compared to adze-eye hammers. And it's positively nosetippy when you compare it to early hammers that have a short neck and a
straight hole through the head.
But don’t be a
---
raid o
---
 vintage hammers. I
---
 the tool 
---
eels good in your

hands — real good — then it's worth keeping. You can wedge the handle tight,
and i
---
 you are a 
---
urniture maker you are unlikely to loosen that connection
in your li
---
etime.

Dead-blow Mallets
Dead-blow mallets are a luxury. You can get by without one. But they are
priced so reasonably that it’s puerile parsimony to eschew them. The previous sentence should prove to you that I indeed have a master’s degree, which

has caused me nothing but trouble at drunk-driving checkpoints (another
story entirely).

Selecting Your Nippers
Be
---
ore you buy a pair o
---

inside and the outside o
---

---
 the tool
to grab nails that are 
---
lush with the wood, but it strengthens the jaws and

prevents them 
---
rom chipping.
Open the nippers and take a look at the inside o
---
 the jaws. The bevel on
the jaws should be crisp, but not too sharp. A sharp bevel will cut the nail
when you mean to pull it. But the bevel needs to be sharp enough so that you

can snip a nail i
---

---

---

---
inger

ugly mark. You can

The right drivers. I

Straight (Slotted) Screwortvers

Keystone or Cabinet?
The tips o
---

---

The keystone tips 
---
lare out in width then taper again up the shank o
---
 the
driver. These are the more common pattern. The cabinet drivers are simply
straight — no taper. These are harder to 
---
ind.

The keystone drivers are 
---
ine 
---
or hardware and installing some screws
when building carcases. Their disadvantage is quickly revealed when you
try to use a keystone bit to sink a screw in a deep counterbore. The 
---

edges o

I don’t use many screws in carcase construction, so keystone drivers are

per

The Tips Width e? Thickness
The most important aspect o
---

o

---

---
it in the

---

most o

---

bigger than 1/4", but I rarely use them

Note that you might see some variation in the real world depending on
the screw. However, usually a 3/16" and a 1/4" driver will handle most screws

that are straight and small.
So it sounds like you need only two straight drivers, right? Well, maybe.
The thickness o

some o

Tip Material
Some tips are too so

Screwdrivers can be made

DUERTRING

21007

more resistant to rust. As to hardness, most specs I've seen place a good
screwdriver tip at somewhere between 58 to 62 on the Rockwell hardness

“C” scale. That makes it harder than a pry bar and a saw and about as hard

as some chisels.
While hardness might seem to be the bee's knees here, any tool that is too

hard can easily shatter. So you want a balance o
---
 toughness and hardness.
In general I think that screwdrivers that are not plated at the tips are

going to be higher quality. That can mean that the tip was precision ground
a
---
ter the tool was plated, or that the manu
---

ing

---

---

A Close Look at the Chuck
There are a nutty, crazy and insane number o

* Curious three-jawed modern chucks. Some hold the square tapered

shanks and some hold only round-shanked bits. As you can probably
guess, I don’t have much love to give the three-jawed chucks on braces
that hold only the round-shanked bits.

The Buck Stops at the Pad
Finally, don’t buy a brace with a pad that wobbles a lot. A little wiggle is OK.
A pad that doesn’t wobble at all is awesome. A tight pad contributes to the

smooth and straight usage o
---
 the tool. Don’t accept a wobbly pad. Move on
to the next $5 tool.

Hand Drill or Drill/Driver
For driving bits that are 1/4" diameter or less, you have two good choices:
a cordless drill/driver or a hand drill. Battery-powered drill/drivers mutate
with the changing seasons. There’s nothing that any book, magazine or website can tell you that is valid except 
---
or this:
The

batteries

eventually go bad and cannot

hold a charge — then it’s usually cheaper to buy

them. You turn the crank and the chuck spins.
There's never anything more to buy.

Vintage hand drills are plenti

better-than-new hand drills. There are new modern-made hand drills that
are wholly adequate but more expensive than vintage models that work just

the same. So it's your decision: Take your time and spend less, or get it done
and spend a little more.

Gearing
A good hand drill has a minimum amount o
---
 slop between the gear that spins
vertically and the gear or gears that spin horizontally. Loose and wobbly

gears result in lost e
---

---
ort. The tighter the gears, the less energy is wasted.
High-quality hand drills will have one vertical gear (the one you spin)

and two horizontal gears (the ones that spin the chuck. Having two horizon-

tal gears reduces the amount o
---
 lost energy in the mechanism. I
---
 you latch
onto a hand drill with only one horizontal gear, don’t 
---
ret. As long as everything runs tight you'll be 
---
ine.

Advanced Gearing
Some drills have a ridiculous number o
---
 options 
---
or spinning the chuck, especially those made by the North Bros. o
---
 Philadelphia. I have one drill 
---
rom

them, the No. 1530A, which has a gear shi
---
t with 
---
ive positions.
In the position closest to the chuck, the drill operates as you would ex-

pect: Spin the wheel clockwise and you drill in. Counterclockwise, drill out.
Position two: Clockwise does nothing. Counterclockwise rotates the

chuck to drill out.
Position three: Clockwise rotates the chuck to drill in. Counterclockwise

does nothing.
Position 
---
our: This is hard to believe until you see it. No matter which
way you turn the wheel, the chuck always spins to drill into the work. Crazy.

Position 
---
ive: The gears are locked, like my brain when I tried to 
---
igure it
out (downloading the 1908 patent papers didn’t help much).

You don’t need this sort o

Dowel Plate and/or Steel Plate 
---
or Clinching Naws

---

Clinching (sometimes spelled “clenching”) is driving a long nail through
two pieces o
---
 woodwork. The nail is so long that it goes through both pieces

o
---
 wood. Its pointy tip is then curled back into the lower piece o
---
 wood. It's
like a nail that is a big old 
---
ish hook. I
---
 properly applied, the 
---
ish isn’t going
to get away, and the two pieces o
---
 wood won't come apart.

There are a variety o
---

I like.

1. You drive the long nail through both pieces o
---
 wood. On the “exit
wound” side o
---
 the joint you have 1/4" to 3/8" o
---
 the nail sticking out. So you
place the assembly onto a steel or iron plate. Position it so the head o
---
 the nail

is on the plate. Strike the tip o
---
 the nail with your hammer. It will curl into a
“J” shape, which you can pound back into the wood.
2. The 
---
ast way to clinch a nail is to bore a pilot hole through both pieces
then place the work on your metal plate. Put the pilot hole over the plate.

Hammer the nail through the holes and into the plate. When the nail hits the
plate it will rebound/curl over, and its tip will turn back into the work. Again,

STRIKING
& FASTENING

TOOLS

Dect

it will look like a 
---
ish hook in the end. When the nail is driven all the way in,

you can turn the work over and see that the tip o
---
 the nail is bent over.
Either way, this is a permanent piece o
---

The other thing you can do with this steel/Aron plate is to turn it into
a “dowel skinner” or “dowel

“Swaging” is when you pound or press the two leaves o
---

they are parallel and touching. Usually butt hinges come 
---
rom the 
---
actory
so the leaves touch only at the tips when closed. I
---

---

huge and ugly reveal (gap) on the hinge side o
---

hinge shrinks this gap to an acceptable distance (1/32" or so).
You can swage a hinge by squeezing the two leaves together in a met-

alworking vise. Or you can beat the gap shut by placing one lea
---

metal plate and tapping the other lea
---

a second plate or chunk o

andsaws are essential to a woodworker. Attempting to build

The Handle, or Tote
Many woodworkers overlook the tote, which is the way that you (Mr. or

---

---
it your hand, then sawing is going to

Handles also have a “hang” angle. I could bore you to sleep with discussions o
---

---

---
e

about this). Here, however, is what you need to know. “Hang” is essentially
how the saw extends 
---
rom your arm. A low hang angle is where your grip
is 90° to the line o
---

easier to control in my opinion.
A high hang angle is when your hand is kicked up 25° to 45° o
---

---

---

---
 the tool, and allows you to stand

more upright while sawing, but it can make the saw a little trickier to steer.

Hang angles were lower in times past. Nowadays most hang angles are

---
airly high. You can get used to either. I learned to saw using higher hang

angles, and that’s what I pre
---

The thickness o

use it. Too-thin sawblades kink easily. Too-thick sawblades are heavy and
require more e

---
or you. Japanese saw plates are thinner —

tensions and strengthens the blade. Western saws are thicker. A handsaw (a
saw without a back) will typically be about .027" to .035" thick or more at
the toothline and get thinner

aS you

up

the

is intentional and reduces

on the teeth.

The Teeth
Western saws have triangular-shaped teeth. Their shape determines how
they cut. I
---
 the tooth leans 
---
orward, then it will be more aggressive, but it

236

TEE

C 1S 30 OO ee OAR IES ch
HH Sil
ANGAVRA

will be harder to start the saw in the cut and keep it moving smoothly. I
---

it leans back, the cut will be slower, but it will be an easier cut to start and
maintain. This characteristic is called “rake.” With saws, the more the tooth
leans back, the more rake it is said to have.
The size o
---
 the teeth is important. I
---
 the teeth are too small, then the gullets between the teeth will 
---
ill up with sawdust and the saw will cut too slow.

I
---
 the tooth is too big, then the saw will jam and the cut will be rough and
splintery. How big should the tooth be?
There are rules o
---
 thumb, but there is so much 
---
lexibility that I think it’s
easier to see what the median is then adjust 
---
rom there.
Dovetail saw specialized 
---
or drawers: 18-20 ppi (points per inch)

Dovetail saw ideal 
---
or carcases: 15 ppi

Carcase saw: 12-14 ppi
Tenon or sash saw: 10-11 ppi

Handsaw/panel saw 
---
or knocking down stock: 7-8 ppi
Handsaw/panel saw 
---
or 
---
ine cuts: 12 ppi
Ripsaw 
---
or 
---
urniture work: 4-5 ppi
These saws and these con
---
igurations are typical 
---
or dealing with stock

between 3/4" and 1" thick, a thickness that is typical 
---
or 
---
urniture. I
---
 you deal

---
iner than

the above numbers. I
---
 you make large-scale 
---
urniture, then you are going to
want saws with teeth that are coarser than above.

But let me say the 
---
ollowing. And it is anti-intellectual. You can make

almost any saw do any job at hand — within reason. A 15 ppi dovetail saw can
do any dovetail chore. A 10 ppi saw can do any tenon chore. An 8 ppi handsaw can do almost any crosscutting chore. It’s only when you do something
ridiculous that you will get spanked.
I once taught a class where a student tried to cut tenon cheeks in 3-1/2"-

ESSENTIAL

SAWS

257

wide stock with a 24-ppi saw. He worked at it 
---
or a good long time. In 
---
act,
all the other students were done with 
---
our tenons and he was still on his 
---
irst

cheek. So there are limits.

One other characteristic o
---
 the teeth is the bevel on the 
---
ront edge o
---
 the
tooth. Pure rip teeth have little or no bevel — they are 
---
iled straight across.

Crosscut teeth are 
---
iled with a bevel — called 
---
leam — up to 20° or so. The

---
leam makes 
---
or a cleaner crosscut because it turns each tooth into a kni
---
e
that slices the grain instead o
---
 pulling it up like a rip tooth.

And the 
---
inal signi
---
icant tooth characteristic is the “set” o
---
 each tooth. Set
is how much each tooth is bent le
---
t or right. The set makes the ker
---
— the slot
lett by the saw —a

little wider than the sawplate, which ensures that the saw

wont jam in the cut when 
---
riction gets involved.

Most saws have too much set, which makes the ker
---
 too large. A large

ker
---

line. I
---

jam about 3/4" into the cut. Game over.

How much set is good? Well i
---

---

hal
---
