 what is typical on saws 
---
rom the 
---
actory.
How do you eliminate set? The best way to reduce the set is to learn to

sharpen the saw with a saw 
---
ile. Every sharpening reduces the amount o
---

set in the teeth. A
---
ter two or three sharpenings, you will have no set on your
teeth and you will probably have to introduce some set be
---
ore your next 
---
il-

ing. The tool you need to do this is a saw-set, a pliers-like device that grabs
one tooth and bends it away 
---
rom you.
There is, actually, quite a lot to know about saw sharpening. So my advice would be to buy a good sharp saw at the outset and use it 
---
or a year or
so until it becomes dull. Then start learning to sharpen. You'll be able to use

your experience with your saw to ensure that your

Early woodworking books don’t make a real distinction between saws

---

---

---

---

The truth is that the tenon saw o

---
or crosscutting the shoulders. A big handsaw or panel saw ripped
the cheeks. That works. Other sources have the cheeks being cut with a tenon saw and a carcase saw being used to saw the shoulders. That works, too.
So you don't have to take a stand on this issue. Since the middle o

---

Here's another historical 
---
act worth pondering: Early tenon saws were

larger than the typical modern tenon saw, up to 18" long. And the blades
were wider — as wide as 4". Big saws are actually easier to keep vertical than
smaller ones. The reason is that the tenon saw has a higher “moment o
---
 inertia.” When you balance a weight in the air (such as the heavy back o
---
 a saw),

it’s easier to 
---
eel when everything is per
---
ectly vertical and maintain it when
the weight is higher up.

So a deep blade on the tenon saw allows you to get the sawplate per
---
ectly
vertical. And a shallow blade with a lower moment o
---
 inertia (such as on a

dovetail saw) allows you to angle the tool easily 
---
or the tail cuts without the
back inter
---
ering with your cut, tipping you too 
---
ar one Way or the other.

This little 
---
act could be evidence that tenon saws were used 
---
or cheeks
because you want vertical cheeks. Or it could be evidence that you wanted
dead-vertical shoulders.

More About the Blade
Because the tenon saw is so big, it’s easy to make the leap that the blade
should be thick. The opposite should be true. Early tenon saws had relatively thin blades, about .026" at times. This makes the saw more 
---
ragile and
sensitive to heat, but it also makes the saw easier to push in thick stock.
Thick tenon saws are much harder to use 
---
or me. I’ve used some that

are .032" and thicker. No matter how sharp they are, it’s a lot o
---
 steel to
push through a big tenon cheek. Thin tenon saws, on the other hand, 
---
loat

through the work, even with the same 
---
iling as a thick one. But the thin
blades can heat up and temporarily warp.

Tenon saws typically have 10 or 11 ppi. When they are used 
---
or sawing

---

---
or rip cuts. Some woodworkers have trouble

sawcut is like landing an airplane. You want a gentle touchdown.

some little saw-

Flush-cut Saw
A 
---

Here it is: Though 
---
lush-cut saws are supposed to have zero set to their
teeth (which allows them to do their job) the truth is more complex. Flushcut saws — at least every one that I have encountered — have set, but only on

one 
---
ace o
---
 the saw.

Why is this? My guess is that the sawmakers create the 
---
lush-cut saw by

doing all their 
---
iling 
---
rom one side o
---

The result is that one 
---
ace is completely 
---
ree o
---
 set and burrs. The other 
---
ace

— which was the 
---
ace where the 
---
ile exited — has some burrs and upset teeth.
The result? Flush-cut saws are usually set to only one side o
---
 the blade.
This can throw some woodworkers. The saw is inconsistent. Sometimes

ESSENTIAL

SAWS

261

Use
---
ul when understood. Flush-cut saws are supposed to have no set, so they won't

scratch the work. Truth is, they have a little set on one side and no set on the other. You
need to get this straight in your head be

ore making critical 
---

it works great. Other times it chews up your wood and the saw dives into

the work at the wrong moment. I
---
you can

igure out which 
---

which does not, you will be ahead o

I do this by observation, with my

that mark (and I have been known to engrave it onto the sawblade with one

able to saw pins and dowels and whatnot

Push or Pull?
As with all modern saws, you can choose between Western-style teeth that

are pushed though the work and Eastern-style teeth that are pulled.
While I like Western teeth, this is one case where I pre

---

There are many tooth con

---

quickly and easily. So much so that some woodworkers

Teeth 
---
rom the East
One last thing about the teeth: Don’t try to apply the same rules o
---
 Western
teeth to Eastern teeth when picking a saw. Pull saws have teeth that are
shaped more like needles than triangles. As a result, they are more 
---
ragile

than Western teeth, but they have much deeper gullets. Because o
---
 these
deep throats, you can use a 
---
iner-pitched saw 
---
or coarse work. So the ppl o
---


the saw isn’t as critical with pull saws as it is with Western saws, except at
the margins when dealing with really thick stock.

There is actually a great deal to learn about the teeth on pull saws. But
because my name is Schwarz — an Axis-power name to be sure, but a West-

ern Axis power — I’m going to 
---
ocus this book on Western teeth. Still, here is
a short primer on things to look 
---
or when buying a pull saw.

The color o
---
 the teeth. Really? Yes, really. Consumer-grade Japanese
saws have teeth that are discolored. They are kind o
---
 black and rainbow-y,
like an oil slick. This discoloration, which occurs only at the toothline o
---

the saw, is a result o
---
 the induction-hardening process. This is where a high

ESSENTIAL

SAWS

25 3

electrical current is passed through the teeth, which makes them extremely
hard. Hard is good because they will stay sharp 
---
or a long time. But hard is

also bad because they will be too hard to resharpen. A 
---

over the sur
---
ace.
Saws with induction-hardened teeth 
---

---
uture. Once they have


---

lighter back to the

---

I

It’s not. But the economic equation here is hard to deny.

The coping saw is unloved, unheralded and under-appreciated. Yet as

Some Evidence
The coping saw is a D-shaped metal 
---
rame saw that is obviously descended

---
rom the early Roman 
---
orms, which 
---
eature a thin blade that is held in ten-

sion by a wooden 
---
rame and string.
The 
---
rame saw came into use 
---
or marquetry, and as this art 
---
orm reached
its zenith in the 17th and 18th centuries, all its tools became specialized
and re
---
ined. André Roubo dedicated an entire volume to its practice, “Le
Menuisier Ebéniste.” And in plate 292, Roubo shows what he calls a “mar-

quetry saw,” which is a metal 
---
rame saw that tensions the blade without a
string or toggle arrangement. At 
---
irst glance the saw looks a lot like what we

call a coping saw, though the blade does not rotate in its 
---
rame and the throat

ESSENTIAL

SAWS

255

o
---
 the tool is quite deep.
The other tool development that seems related to the coping saw is the
development o
---
 similar D-shaped 
---
rame saws that were used to cut metal or
exotic materials, such as jeweler’s saws, the hack saw, the piercing saw and

the ivory saw. These saws show up in 18th-century plates (such as Roubo’s)

and become 
---
airly common in the 19th century.
So by the 19th century here’s what we have: Marquetry saws with deep
throats (sometimes deep enough to warrant an NC-17 rating), and 
---
rame

saws with shallow throats used 
---
or cutting dense materials. The coping saw
appears to be a tool that bridges these two 
---
orms.

First, what's the deal with its name? “Coping” is a 17th-century term
(thank you, “Ox
---
ord English Dictionary”) that re
---
ers to the top course o
---


bricks or blocks on a wall. I
---
 the bricks were beveled to help shed rain, they
were called “coping blocks.” So the term “coping” was clearly related to a

beveled edge.
In modern woodworking, coping can re
---
er to removing the bevel 
---
rom a

mitered piece o
---
 moulding in order to 
---
it two pieces o
---
 moulding in an inside
corner. The term coping is also used concerning doors that have their inside

edges moulded. The ends o
---
 the door’s rails are “coped” so they nest against
the moulding on the door’s stiles. So “coping” is actually the act o
---
 cutting the
negative shape o
---
 the moulding on a piece.
The 
---
irst re
---
erences to a “coping saw” appear, as best as I can tell, in

the 19th century in books and tool catalogs. The 
---
irst U.S. patent 
---
or a saw
that quacks like a modern coping saw is an 1883 application 
---
rom William

Jones — earlier patented 
---
rame saws look to me like marquetry saws with

deep throats.
That 1883 patent called the tool a “saw 
---
rame 
---
or a jeweler’s saw.” The

---
ollowing year, C.A. Fenner patented a mechanism that allowed the blade to
rotate in the 
---
rame (it’s amazing in its gizmosity). He called it (most unhelp-


---
ully) a “hand saw.”

256

THE

ANAR

OHS

SiO

Os

Cri

sil

And in 1887, Christopher Morrow patented a tool called a “coping saw,”
which uses a blade-tensioning mechanism more like a wooden bowsaw. A
---
ter that point, the term “coping saw” crops up regularly in catalogs and pat-

ent 
---
ilings. By 1900, the saw is everywhere.
The inexpensive tool became a ubiquitous part o
---
 the carpenter's tool
kit. It also became a tool that was central to the manual training movement
o
---
 the late 19th century (what we call shop class). The coping saw was used
by students to cut out all manner o
---
 toys and decorative objects. And many
books, manuals and patterns devoted to coping saw work appear in the late

19th and early 20th centuries.

Used Everywhere, But Not tn the Same Way
Though lots o
---
 people big and small were using the coping saw, there was
(and still is) a major disagreement about it: Should it cut on the push stroke
(like most Western saws) or the pull stroke (like most Eastern saws)?
The earliest source

I could 
---
ind that addressed the matter directly was

“Trade Foundations,” a pre-vocational textbook 
---
rom 1919 published by the
Guy M. Jones Co.

“Most coping saw work is done with the work resting horizontally on the
bench and held in place with the le
---
t hand. The teeth should point toward
the saw handle. When the vise is used to hold the work, the saw teeth should

point away 
---
rom the handle.”

When I looked to other writers who were traditionally trained, their
opinions seemed to support this early view. Robert Wearing, in “The Essen-

tial Woodworker,” shows a coping saw cutting on the push to remove waste

between dovetails (the work is in a vise). He states that coping saws work on
the push, except on a horizontal saw table when working with thin material.
Then it should cut on the pull.

Charles Hayward, in “Tools 
---
or Woodwork,” states that coping saws are
generally used on the push, but there are occasions when the blade should

ESSENTIAL

SAWS

26:7

be reversed.
Among the modern writers, many (with the exception o
---
 Aldren A. Wat-

son) seem to pre
---
er to use the saw on the pull stroke only.
In “Carpentry & Construction” by Rex Miller and Glenn E. Baker, the
authors state that the teeth should point toward the handle. “This means it

cuts only on the downward stroke.”
In the “Band Saw Handbook,” Mark Duginske writes that the coping
saw is used only on the pull stroke. “Because the blade is cutting on the pull
stroke, the blade tensions itsel
---
.”
So with all this divided opinion, I think it’s best to 
---
ile this debate away
with the other unanswerable questions.

The Weak Modern Form
Now we come to the real reason I've been digging through all this old paper. Modern coping saws are — 
---
or the most part — 
---
lawed. But they weren't

always this bad.
What's wrong with them? Mostly it’s the mechanism that allows you to
rotate the blade. I’ve never had a coping saw that could hold its setting — no

matter how much I tightened the 
---
rame or even how many lock washers I
added to the saw.

A
---
ter a certain number o
---
strokes, the blade goes into wind — meaning the
blade rotates more (or less) at the toe than at the heel. This warping makes

the saw hard to control and is one o
---
 the reasons why some dovetail savants
use a jeweler’s saw and tweak the blades manually with pliers.
Me, I’m done with jeweler’s saws, which I suspect are made only to sell

the easily snapped blades.
So it sounds like we should get the CAD jockeys to jump on this problem, right? Maybe not. As I was browsing through my old tool catalogs and
patent 
---
ilings, I 
---
ound all manner o
---
 mechanisms 
---
or 
---

---

blades in wind. Some o
---

The Essential Coping Saw
A coping saw has to be able to do just a 
---
ew things really well. Most modern

ones 
---
ail on all three points. Here’s my short list. A coping saw should:
1. Hold the blade in tension so you can use it either on the pull stroke or
the push stroke.
2. Allow the blade to be rotated 360° and hold that position during a cut,

both at the toe and the heel.
3. Have a com
---
ortable handle 
---
or pushing or pulling the tool.

Present e° Future Tense
The heart o
---
a coping saw is its tensioning mechanism. A low-rent saw makes

the blade twang like a wet noodle when you pluck it in the 
---
rame. I
---

doesn't ping like the high “E” string on a guitar, it will buckle as you are cutting and wander o
---

---

orce a saw to tension the blade by bending

the

---

will 
---
orget that tension.

A good coping saw 
---

---
unction. I have 100-year-old coping saws (some that were unused, some
that were used heavily) with 
---

---

and materials are everything.

As 
---

---
or a saw that will allow you to over-tension
the saw. I
---

probably should pass on the saw.
As

Handle
The handle o
---
 a coping saw is more than just a stick. In 
---
act, i
---

just a stick then you might not be happy with it over the long term. Why?

Because the handles can be optimized 
---
or both pulling and pushing with just
a small alteration. Or the handles can 
---
ollow the pattern o
---
 modern coping
saws and be optimal 
---
or neither.

A good coping saw handle will have a small swelling up by the 
---
errule
or the 
---
rame (not all saws have a 
---

---
 metal around the

handle). This swelling is quite use
---
ul. When you are pushing the saw, you

place your thumb and index 
---

tool without gripping the handle 
---
iercely.
When you are pulling the saw back, the swelling is an excellent place to

put your 
---
ingers to pull the saw back toward you as you cut. In other words,
the swelling is ideal, whether you orient the blade 
---
or pull-cut action or pushing.

In addition to the swelling, consider the material. Is the handle made

---
rom wood or plastic or worse? Wood is ideal. It is warm and can be easily

shaped to 
---
it your hand. Plastic or rubber handles might 
---
eel good at 
---
irst, but
they can get slimy or tough as you break them in. Wood, on the other hand,
gets better the more you use it.
One last detail on the handle: I
---

the pull stroke, don’t be surprised i
---

---

---
 the saw. This has

happened to me a couple times. The best solution is to drill a hole through

the 
---
errule and handle and drive a small steel pin through the hole. File the
pin 
---
lush.

Good gaps. A skip-tooth blade has smaller teeth and a big gullet. This gives you a smooth

I

ppi-sized tooth. The result is a big old gullet between the teeth. So you get a

with that blade con

Types o

Blade Ends
For the most part, modern coping saw blades have pins that bisect the blade

stock. The pins hook into the blade-holding mechanism and allow you to

---

---
. Some blades are

---

---
lat. But these aren't copingsaw blades. They are jeweler’s saw blades. A jeweler’s saw is like a coping

---

---

---
erent

clamp almost anything in the saw 
---

coping saw.

st?

---
ollow it?

¢ Use minimal downward pressure. Allow the saw’s weight to carry the
cut. Downward

pressure brings more

hand and arm

muscles into the

equation. And tensing those muscles will steer the saw le
---
t or right.
e Imagine the saw as longer than it is. This will 
---
ool you into using longer
strokes, which will allow you to saw 
---
aster and wear your teeth evenly.

¢ Whenever possible, advance on two lines. For example, when crosscutting a board, saw diagonally through the thickness until you have

reached the bottom 
---
ace o
---
 the board. Then 
---
ocus on sawing the line
on the top 
---
ace. Your diagonal strokes will guide the saw as you move
through the board.

Always work right against a line. Never saw a certain distance away

---
rom a line. You will never become a good sawyer i
---
 you don't try to
snuggle right up against the line. Instead, you will become a good chiseler as you pare your way to your line.

---
ting the saw a tad on the return stroke helps clear your line o
---
 saw-

dust.

267

he simplest thing in woodworking — getting a keen edge — has been
made impossibly complex by the crazy number o
---
 sharpening stones

and guides in catalogs and stores. Anyone can learn to sharpen a
passable edge with about 30 minutes o
---
 personal instruction. But a
---
ter that
incredible experience, you are then set on a never-ending road o
---
 making
your next edge better than your last.

Thanks to my job at the magazine, I have set up and sharpened more

tools than anyone I know. I personally have taught a 
---
ew hundred people
how to sharpen. I sharpen every single day. Yet, my edges still improve every

year.
When you pick a sharpening system, think o
---
 it as an old-
---
ashioned wedding. You should devote yoursel
---
 to one system. Spurn all others. Try to

explore the system you picked in immense detail. Take good care o
---
 your
sharpening medium. Keep it clean. Keep things true. Keep everything ready
to go at all times. Do these things, and the rewards will be immense.
Jumping 
---
rom system to system, jig to jig, is the road to ruin. Every

sharpening medium (waterstone, oilstone, ceramic, sandpaper, diamonds) is
di
---

---
erent, and each reveals its secrets over time.
So here’s the big question: Which system should you choose?

Answer: It doesn’t matter.
Every system works. I’ve used them all and each is a trade-o
---

---
 between
sharpening speed, maintenance and expense. There is not one system that is

---
ast, easy to maintain and cheap. People will tell you that there is (because

they are married to their system), but they are blinded by devotion. And I'm

OK with that.
Though the sharpening systems might seem wildly di
---

---
erent, they all
have three 
---
unctions in common. They are used to grind, hone and polish

your tools. Grinding is when you remove a lot o
---
 metal quickly to 
---
ix an edge
that needs repair or needs to be taken back to a 
---
actory state a
---
ter lots o
---

sharpening jobs. It is in
---
requent and generally avoided.

---
ond o
---
 them and their cutting characteristics. They are a little slow, but | can be that way at times, too.

Honing is where you usually begin your sharpening job. Honing removes

the old worn edge and starts a new edge. Honing also is the stage where you
typically change the shape o
---
 an edge. You might adda slight curve to your

block plane blade. Or you might straighten out the edge o
---
 a chisel that was
a little crooked 
---
rom your last honing.
Polishing is where you re
---
ine the honed edge until you get to whatever
insane grit level you desire. The more polish you impart, the more durable
the edge — but there is a de
---
inite point o
---
 diminishing returns. For some
woodworkers, one polish stone is all they will ever need their entire lives.

For others, they will need 
---
ive or six polish stones to be truly happy.

So let's discuss the trade-o
---

---
s 
---
or the common systems. There are lots o
---

other systems out there, but 
---
or the last 100 years or so, these are the ones
that most people have used.

Oulstones: Slow, Steady e° Durable
For the last century or so, most Western woodworkers have used oilstones.

These stones occur naturally, though most oilstones these days are manu
---
actured using a process that bonds the grit together like making a brick.
Oilstones are lubricated with oil. And almost any oil will do, 
---
rom WD40 to mineral oil to 3-in-1 light machine oil. The oil is a plus because it won't
rust your tools and it (usually) evaporates slowly. So you can squirt and go

and not have to constantly re-apply oil.
Oilstones cut slowly compared to most other sharpening systems, so you
are going to need to use more strokes to hone and polish your edge. But the

upside to the slow cutting action is that the oilstones also wear slowly, so you

don’t need to 
---
latten the stones very o
---
ten. In 
---
act, some woodworkers report
that they have never 
---
lattened their oilstones.

That has not been my experience. Oilstones work better i
---
 they are 
---
lat.
They cut 
---
aster because 
---
lattening them exposes 
---
resh abrasive and prevents
them 
---
rom glazing. And 
---
lat stones are predictable. You'll never have to remember where a low spot is that could ruin an edge. | 
---
latten my oilstones
with a diamond plate, which is 
---

---
icient.

Stropping
Stropping and oilstones go hand in hand. The reason 
---
or that is that the 
---
inest
oilstone (the translucent) is still a little coarse 
---
or some tools. So to get the
keenest edge, you have to strop. Stropping isn’t some weird magical process.

ESSENTIAL

KIT

Daan

It’s just a 
---
ine polishing process. The strop itsel
---
 is like the stone. The polishing compound is a waxy abrasive with very small abrasive particles, as small

as | micron, that impart a nice polish on the cutting edge.
When you strop, you work the tool in only one direction: pulling the tool

toward you. I
---
 you push the tool 
---
orward, the sharp edge will likely dig into
the leather, damaging your strop.

Waterstones use a cheap and common lubricant — water. And that would

seem to be an advantage. But water is the compound that encourages iron
and steel to become rust. So you have to keep an eye on the water and wipe
down every tool with oil a

---

Burnisher
I

Workbench
I have written two books on workbenches

that agonize over the details o
---


joinery, wood selection, vises and so on. But i
---
 you don’t want to buy those

books, here is the down-and-dirty data you need to pick a bench design.
Note: Be
---
ore you write me an angry letter about this list — you don't have

to have a good bench to be a good woodworker. You can work on the kitchen
table and make world-class work. But a good bench will make many operations easier. It’s simply a tool: the biggest clamp in the shop.

ESSENTIAL

APPLIANCES

2956

My daughter's bench. This French-style workbench has yet to 
---
ail me. It is sturdy, heavy
and easily holds boards when working their 
---
aces, edges and ends.

Rule No. 1: Always Add Mass
For workbenches, here’s my maxim: I
---
 it looks stout, then make it doubly so.

Everything about a workbench takes punishment that is akin to a kitchen
chair in a house 
---
ull o
---
 8-year-old boys.
Early Roman workbenches were built like a Windsor chair. Stout legs

were tenoned into a massive top and wedged in place. Traditional French
workbenches had massive tops (up to 6" thick), with legs that were big

enough to be called tree trunks. Later workbenches relied more on engineering than mass. The classic Continental-style workbench uses a trestle design

and dovetails in the aprons and vises to create a bench 
---
or the ages. The
19th-century English workbench uses an early torsion-box design to create

a stable place to work. And good-quality modern workbenches use threaded
rods and bolts to tighten up a design that lacks mass.
Many inexpensive commercial benches are ridiculously rickety. They

296

THE

ANDRCH
ITS TS

TOOLRSGCH

ES T

sway and rack under hand pressure. You can push them across your shop by
per
---
orming simple operations: routing, sawing, planing. I
---
 the bench looks
delicate or its components are sized like a modern dining table, I would take
a closer look be
---
ore committing.

A big thick top and stout legs add mass that will help your work. Heavy
cabinet saws with lots o
---
 cast iron tend to run smoother than plastic ones.
The same goes with benches. Once your bench hits about 300 pounds, it
won't move unless you want it to move.

Rule No. 2: Use Stout Joints
Overbuild your workbench by using the best joints. These are times to whip
out the through-tenon and dovetail. I
---
 you 
---
ollowed rule No. 1, then rule No.

2 should be no problem. Your joints will be sized to 
---
it the massive scale o
---


your components. I
---
 you cannot rely on mass, then you should bee
---
 things up
with superior joinery. While dovetails and through-tenons are overkill 
---
or a
towel rack, they are de rigueur 
---
or a bench.

That's because you are applying wracking 
---
orce to the workbench with
typical operations and your vises will do their best to tear apart your bench.
All wooden vises need to be overbuilt or they will sel
---
-destruct when you
cinch them down hard. I’ve even seen a vise rip a benchtop 
---
rom its base.

Make your tenons thick and your mortises deep. I
---
 you know how to
drawbore a mortise-and-tenon joint, this is one good application. Have you

ever been in a timber-
---
ramed barn? Did you look at the joints? They’re massive and pegged. Imitate that.

I think benches are a good place to practice your skills at cutting these
classic joints, but some woodworkers still resist. I
---
 that’s you, you should
investigate hardware to strengthen your bench. Threaded rods, bed bolts,
special bench bolts or even stove bolts can turn a spindly assembly into
something rigid that can be snugged up i
---
 it loosens. The hardware won't
give you mass, but it will strengthen a rickety assembly.

ESSENTIAL

APPLIANCES

ZO.

Permanent. The sliding dovetail and through-tenon on this French bench makes a bench
where the base and top act as one.

Rule No. 5: Pick Your Wood Based on Its Sti
---

---
ness, Not Its Spectes
Use a sti
---

---
, inexpensive and common wood to build your bench. Showcase
benches made 
---
rom exotic materials are nice. But 
---
ocus on the 
---
unctions

be
---
ore the 
---
lash. I’d rather have a construction-lumber bench that 
---
ollowed
all these rules than a beauti
---
ul European beech bench that skipped even one

o
---
 these concepts.

Rule No. 5: TheOverall Dimensions o
---
 Your Bench Are Critical
Your bench design cannot be too heavy or too long. But its top can easily

be too wide or too tall. I think your benchtop should be as long as possible.
Find the wall where your workbench will go. Hint: Pick the wall that has a
window. Measure that space. Subtract 4' 
---
rom that measurement, and that’s
a good length 
---
or the top. Note: The benchtop must be at least 5' long unless

you build only small-scale items. Furniture-sized parts typically range up to
48" long and you want to support these 
---
ully with a little room to spare.

I've made tops that are 8' long. It is di
---

---
icult to imagine a workbench that
is too long. The same thing almost goes 
---
or thickness. It is the thickness that
allows the top to be that long. I
---
 you make the top really thick (4" or a tad

more), then it will o
---

---
er unerring support and allow you to build your bench
without any support system beneath. The top can perch on the legs and will

not sag under its own weight.

The width is a di
---

---
erent matter. You can have a bench that is too wide

---
or a one-person shop. I’ve worked on benches that are 36" wide, and they

have downsides. For starters, i
---
 you park them against the wall you'll have
to stretch to reach the tools hanging on the wall. I
---
 you assemble projects

on your bench, you will 
---
ind yoursel
---
 dancing around it a lot more than you

should.
But there’s more. Cabinetwork is sized in standard chunks. These sizes
come 
---
rom the human body; they aren’t arbitrary. A kitchen’s base cabinet is

generally 24" deep and 34-12" high. This is important 
---
or a couple reasons.
First: It means you don't really need a bench that’s much more than 24"
deep to build cabinets. With that 24" depth (or even narrower), you actually
get some advantages, including the 
---
act that you can clamp the cabinet to

your bench 
---
rom as many as three sides. That's handy. A deep bench allows
you to clamp your cabinets to the bench on only two sides (with a couple
exceptions). Here's the other thing to keep in mind: Kitchen cabinets are
themselves a highly studied work sur
---
ace. There's a good reason that kitchen

300

THE

ANARCHIST Ss ©TOOT

CHEST

cabinets are 24" deep. And it’s the same reason you don’t want your work-

bench much deeper either.
Now I'm not going to argue with you i
---

---

---

a bench that you share with another woodworker 
---
acing you; you might
need more depth. But i
---

power
---
ul and right-sized tool.
On the issue o

and there are a wide variety o

must

I wish there were. Some people like low benches; some like them high.

So consider the

---

or wooden handplanes that dictates a low bench).

Here is my experience with bench height: I started with a bench that
was 36" high, which seemed right 
---
or someone who is 6’ 3-58" tall. And 
---
or
machine woodworking I was right. The high bench brought the work close
to my eyes. I loved it. Then my passion 
---
or handwork emerged.

I
---

with a jack plane and a 
---
ew smoothing planes. They worked OK with a high
bench, but I became 
---
atigued quickly.

A
---
ter reading the screeds on bench heights, I lowered the height o
---

36" bench. It seemed radical, but one day I got the nerve up and sawed 2" o
---

---

the legs. Those two inches changed my attitude toward planing.

The 34"-bench height allowed me to use my long leg muscles to propel
the plane 
---
orward instead o
---

Now, be
---
ore you build your next bench at 34" high, stop 
---
or a minute.
That might not be right 
---
or you. Do you use wooden-stock planes? I
---

you
need to consider that the wooden body planes can hold your arms about

to 4" higher o

Rule No. 6: Benches Must Hold the Work in Three Ways
All benches should be able to grip the wood so you can easily work on the


---
aces, the ends and the edges. Many commercial benches 
---
ail on this point.
Submit your bench to what I call the Kitchen Cabinet Door Test. Imag-

ine a typical kitchen door that is 3/4" thick, 15" wide and 23" long. How
would you a
---

---
ix that door 
---
lat on your bench to level its joints then sand (or
plane) it 
---
lat? How would you clamp the door so you could work on the ends

to trim the top rail and tops o
---

---
it its opening? And
how would you secure that door on edge so you can rout its hinge mortise
and plane o
---

---

---
lopping around? Does

THE

VA NAR

your bench pass this test? OK, now ask the same questions with a door that
is 3/4" x 15" x 38". And then try a board that is 3/4" x 12" x 6'.

How you accomplish each o
---

---
unctions is up to you and your
taste and budget. To work on the 
---
aces o
---

a grippy sanding pad, a tail vise with dogs, clamps or hold-downs.

To work on the ends o
---

---
or dovetailing), a metal quick-release vise, a leg vise or a twin-screw vise.
And you can use all o
---

The vise holds one corner o
---

---

Rule No. 7: Make Your Bench Friendly to Clamps
Your bench is a three-dimensional clamping sur
---
ace. Anything that inter
---
eres with clamping work to your benchtop (aprons, a drawer bank, doors,
supports etc.) can make some operations a challenge.

We had a phase at Popular Woodworking where we tried to design a cupholder into every project. It started innocently with a deck chair. Who
doesn’t want a cool beverage at hand out on the deck? Then there was the
dartboard. What goes better with darts than beer? We 
---
inally came to our
senses when designing a series o
---
 cupholders into a Gustav Stickley Morris
chair reproduction. Do you really need a Big Gulp-sized hole in your
chair? I didn’t think so.

Morris

ESSENTIAL

APPLIANCES

503

No skirts. A thin and wide skirt on a workbench will impede your clamping e
---

---
orts at every
turn. A 
---
lat underside is a nice 
---
eature, no matter what sort o
---

The point o
---

I personally 
---
ind troubling. It’s a knee-jerk reaction to a common American

complaint: We don’t think we have enough space in our shops to store our

tools and accessories. And how do we solve this problem with our workbenches? By designing them like kitchen cabinets with a countertop work

sur
---

Rule No. 9: No Fancy Fintshes

Dial Caliper
I end up using this tool quite a bit, especially when thicknessing my stock —

either by hand or by power. The dial caliper can point to high or low spots

---
aster than a ruler. It can serve as a handy depth gauge 
---
or mortises. It can

help you sort out the exact size o
---

---
your drill bits.

It also is use
---
ul 
---
or 
---

---

---
it in its
dado, I'll measure the dado and the shel
---
. I
---
 the shel
---

the dado is wide and I know that my smoothing plane is taking about a
.002"-thick shaving, then I know I have to make at least three passes with
the tool to get the shel
---

I pre
---
er analog dial calipers that read out in decimal inches. Yes, it takes
a little while to learn the decimal equivalents o
---

measurements.

But that’s not why I like them. In hand-tool work, things are rarely at ex-

actly 5/8" or whatever. They are several thousandths o
---

the other o
---

---

using simple subtraction and addition o
---

---
ractions
(especially 64ths) is lunacy, so I avoid the 
---
ractional calipers.

Sharpening a beading plane is pretty easy. I
---

---

---
 the iron

---

---

---

be poker straight. I
---

Once you get the sole and iron tuned up, then you can test the wedge. Yes,

there can be serious problems with the wedge. I
---
 it doesn’t 
---
it the mortise in

---

I
---

---

---

GOOD-TO-HAVE

TOOLS

iy ES)

there is some wood in the mortise or on the wedge that is getting in the way.

You need to remove the wood to get a good 
---
it.
When I run into this problem, I darken the wedge with pencil lead then
drive it into the plane. Then I'll remove it and see where the lead has been

rubbed away on the wedge. This is a high spot. I'll check the wedge with a
straightedge. Does the spot appear high on the wedge? I
---
 yes, then I'll try
trimming it back with a scraper or a chisel. I
---
 it doesn’t look high on the
wedge then I'll look to the mortise in the plane body. A 
---
loat or a chisel can

help remove the high area.
Then I test the wedge again and repeat this process until it will hold

when in use.

A couple beading planes will take care o

 you

Small Ovolo or Ogee Moulding Plane
Once you master the beading plane, then you are ready to move

up toa

small complex moulder, such as a small ovolo or ogee moulding plane. These
have all the same challenges and rewards as a beading plane. When you buy
vintage examples, the irons can be misshapen, the stocks can be warped, and
the wedges might 
---
ly out o
---
 their mortises in use.

These small complex moulders are use
---
ul in producing runs o
---

below, their chie

---

moulding you can draw. The chie

that it is almost impossible to produce the exact same moulding twice. Each

is a little unique, owing to the 
---
ree-
---

---

---

The small complex moulders are great

ing that is used all the time in cabinet work. Small beads, ogees or ovolos are
great

$21

cut until you tell them to stop.

Drawbore Pins, One Pair
Drawboring is a traditional joinery technique that has 
---
allen on hard times. It was
once 
---
airly common
many

early

on

---

Then

are outstanding tools i
---

o
---

The technique involves boring a hole
through a mortise.

Through with you. Mortise 
---
loats

boring a hole

the crisp corners necessary 
---
or a
good 
---
it.

through the matching tenon that is slightly
o
---

---
set toward the shoulder, perhaps 1/16" or a little more. Then a wooden
peg is driven through the assembled joint and the peg pulls the tenon into

the mortise.
The technique is great 
---
or cases in which you don’t have clamps that
are long enough to span the entire length o
---
 the joint. Or perhaps you are
working with wood that is a little wet and you ate concerned about things

shrinking a bit. A drawbored joint can help keep things tight. Or maybe you
aren't sure about the glue you are using. Perhaps you are working without

glue because the wood is too wet.
In these cases, drawboring is a big help.

The risk when drawboring a joint is that, when you drive the peg through
the o
---

---
set holes, it will sel
---
-destruct. And that is where the drawbore pin
comes into play.

It’s an ancient tool that was designed to be pushed into the joint be
---
ore

it o

wooden toggle. The blade is longer (12" is typical 
---
or an English bowsaw)

and the throat is deeper than on a 6" coping saw. This allows you to cut
curves deeper into boards than a coping saw will.

Like all 
---
rame saws, there is a lot o
---
 debate swirling about them. Should

they cut on the pull or push stroke? Are they used with one or two hands?
How much tension do they require?

The answers depend on the user. As with a coping saw, pushing or pull-

ing the blade gives you di
---

---
erent results. Orient the blade to get the result
you want.
Many woodworkers use the tool with one hand or both hands on the

---
ront handle o
---

Saw Vise
To sharpen your saws you need a vise to grip the tool securely by the tooth-

line. A good saw vise will prevent the saw

Trammel Points
Trammel points are the oversized cousins o
---

---

I happen to love curves, so I use

5357

Switching 
---
rom Money to Time
Laboring hard without a pro
---
it motive is disconcerting to some people. We
expect to receive money when we work, so that we can spend it when we are

at rest. Woodworking has taught me to reverse that equation. I build things

in my 
---
ree time so I can avoid buying things. And i
---
 I don’t have to buy as
many things, I don’t have to take on jobs that I don’t like. Then I have more

time available in my shop.
Here's another way I look at it: As I get older, I simply have less desire


---
or money and more desire 
---
or time. This mental shi
---
t might be just the early
40s realization that I’m mortal, but I think it’s more pro
---
ound. I am now
an incredible tightwad with my time. I get angry during con
---
erence calls at
work where people posture, show o
---

---
 or waste my time because they didn’t
do their homework. Waiting in a long line at a grocery raises my blood pressure. Sitting in tra
---

---
ic makes me nuts.

This might sound like I just need a yoga class or a really cleansing chant.
But I'm not an impatient man. My anxiety is merely despair that I am wasting time that could be better spent building, writing or cooking.

I want to make things that are permanent, inspiring or just chewy. And
because my head is on backward when it comes to modern capitalism I want
time more than I want money.

The idea o
---
 time as being the ultimate currency might seem modern or
radical, but it’s neither. It’s not anti-
---
ree market or socialist, and it doesn’t require women to stop shaving their arm pits or grow cotton in the back yard.
Removing money 
---
rom the equation is an idea that has actually worked in

a 
---
ree-market economy just 
---
ine. In 
---
act, one o
---
 the most interesting social

---
ew minutes 
---
rom

I live in Northern Kentucky, which is a 
---
our-minute drive 
---
rom the intersection o
---
 Fi
---
th and Elm streets in downtown Cincinnati. I
---

today, you would 
---

which is a 19th-century repository o
---

At this particular intersection there are now two modern high-rise hotels,
the city’s convention center and a 1980s-era mixed-use development that has

a reputation 
---
or housing doomed businesses.
Yet this street corner in 1827 is where Josiah Warren, a musician, printer
and inventor, opened the Cincinnati Time Store.

The Time Store sold a wide variety o
---
 goods, but it didn’t accept currency. Instead the legal tender was the customer's time. All o
---
 the Time Store's
goods were priced at the cost to produce them, plus a small administration

---
ee (4 percent

to 7 percent)

to keep the store

running,

plus an additional

charge that was based on the time the customer spent haggling 
---
or the goods

with the shopkeeper.

The motto o
---
 the store was: “Cost the limit o
---
 price.” What that meant can
best be explained like this: Say you wanted to buy 12 pounds o
---
 corn 
---
rom

the Time Store. The price was one hour o
---
 your labor — an astonishingly low
price, even now — plus the small administrative 
---
ee and any time you spent

wasting the clerk's time. You would take the corn and be issued a note and
be called upon to do the hour o
---
 labor to pay 
---
or the corn.
As a result o
---
 this unique system, prices at the Cincinnati Time Store

were considerably lower than those at other merchants in the city. Soon
another competing store opened up using the same principles (a
---
ter the shop
owner consulted with Warren 
---
or advice on setting up the competing store).
And other merchants began lowering prices or accepting labor as a means
o
---
 currency. This success
---
ul system worked 
---
or several years (and in other

344

THE

ANAR@GHIST S POOLeCHES T

cities, including Boston) until Warren proved to himsel
---
 that it worked and

closed up shop to put his e
---

---
orts toward a di
---

---
erent social experiment.
Prices were cheaper at Warren's Cincinnati Time Store because all o
---

the middle sellers and distributors had been eliminated. And poor customers
had the same currency as richer ones — an hour o
---
labor was an hour o
---
labor.
In other words, your time got you what you wanted or needed. It saved
you a step — having to convert your time into money to buy an object.

I look at the dining table I built 
---
or our house, and I see 40 hours o
---

---

woodworking is about — making stu

Let's look at those 40 hours another way. Be
---

or a media corporation and

---

| made

or the company.

But i
---

complex and the money would become more diluted. That $515 would end
up split among the 
---

---

---

the 
---
urniture 
---
actory and the people who actually made the table. My mon-

ey wouldn't reward making something as much as it would reward moving
something or storing it until it could be sold.

And I should add that a 40-hour table

rom a

Aesthetic Anarchism
As I extinguished my desires one by one, the process began to shape the

way I looked at the broader world. What do you call it when you want to
make quality things instead o
---

---
or supporting people who were also individual makers 
---
ocused on quality,
permanence and actually making something?

I didn’t have a name

parallel universes. The West side o
---

---

When the West and Schwarz

younger and equally intriguing sister, Kate.

The anarchist’s joint. |
---

---
urniture, you need to use the best joinery available.

Anarchy
&
Cra
---
tsmanship
I
---
 you are going to stop buying commercial 
---
urniture, then you cannot imi-

tate industrial processes. You must pick your joints with care.
When I built my dining table, each joint had to be robust in order 
---
or it to
endure daily and hard use. All the joints in the base are drawbored mortise-

and-tenon joints that are wedged and pegged 
---
or good measure.
I
---

just bought a table at Value City Furniture and called it 
---
uture 
---
irewood.

Those screws are temporary, and will last only a 
---
ew years in a trestle table.
I’m not saying you should never use a biscuit, screw or dowel. Instead,
you need to pick joints that are designed 
---
or the rest o
---
your li
---
e, i
---

---
or-

ever. In a wooden chair or dining table, that means you need to use well-
---
it
mortise-and-tenon joints. Period. No exceptions.

So buy good tools. Because good tools tend to subvert the greater economy, they should be illegal.

Anarchy
e° Tool Chests
By owning your own tools, you are thumbing your nose at the dehumanizing
concept o
---
labor specialization. By rejecting the mass-manu
---
actured style-o
---
the-moment dining tables, you are questioning the dominant institutions that

produce them. By 
---
illing your home with 
---
urniture that will last 
---
or generations, you are knee-capping the system that requires constant consumption.

I call that anarchy. But even i
---
 you don’t call it that (and I don’t recommend that you do) you need a proper tool chest.

Like all important objects and lasagna, you cannot buy a good one. (Unless you get lucky and 
---
ind a good vintage chest at an auction; I don’t recommend vintage lasagna.)

So you need to
devote about 40 to
80 hours to make a

chest that is suitable

---
or a li
---
etime.

The

joinery

be

should

top-shel
---
. The chest
in

this

book

has

more than 100 dove-

tail joints. The chest’s design should be tested and traditional — it should look
as appealing in 100 years as it does the day you 
---
inish it. And it should be the

ideal place to store a complete set o
---
 tools without taking up too much space

and without making the tools di
---

---
icult to 
---
etch when needed.
I
---

---

---
ew. I
---

---

compiled a
---
ter looking at dozens o

---

traditional chest I built in the 1990s.

The Rules 
---
or a Good Tool Cheat
Just like when building a piece o
---
 woodwork, there are guidelines when
building a chest that you ignore at your own peril. Some o
---
 these are more
important than others. And many o
---
 them are interrelated, especially the
guidelines 
---
or materials and joinery.
These rules aren't in any book (until now). They are things that I’ve

culled 
---
rom looking at historic chests in person and on paper. But most o
---

all, they come 
---
rom my own experience. About 1998, I built a traditional
tool chest with a 
---
ancy veneered till based on the chest o
---
 Benjamin Seaton.
I started with that well-established 18th-century 
---
orm. I've used it just about

every day since 1998. And I modi
---
ied it and observed the things about that
chest that I like and hate.

Rule No. 1: As Long as Your Tool Plus Some
First, let’s talk about some o
---
 the concepts that are cut and dried. The size
o
---
 tool chests 
---
or joiners and cabinetmakers is 
---
airly standard. The length o
---

tool chests is typically somewhere between 35" and 43". This dimension al-

lows the chest to easily hold the handsaws used by a joiner, which have a 26"long blade plus another 5" o
---
 wooden tote. Ripsaws can have an even longer
blade, up to 30". Plus the length o
---
 the chest should give you some room to

get your hand in there to grab the tote o
---
 your longest saw.
In addition to long saws, the chest needs to hold the long jointer plane,

one o
---
 the core tools. While metal planes top out at about 24" long, woodenbodied planes can go much longer — 30" long isn’t uncommon. And a chest

longer than 43” will make it harder to transport in a carriage or minivan.

So when you are trying to sketch out the length o
---
 your chest, measure
your longest saw, add 5" so you can get your hand in there easily, and don’t

---
orget to add a couple inches 
---
or the thickness o
---
 the material. That will easily get you to 37" to 40" i
---
 you use 
---
ull-size saws.

BASIC-CHEST

DESIGN

369

Put your right hand in. Secure your body against the rim o
---
 the chest with your o
---

---
-hand.
Root around 
---
or the tool you need with your dominant hand. No deep-knee bends neces-

sary.

The shell o
---
 my chest ended up at 38" long. I want to be able to hold 
---
ullsize handsaws (mine are about 31" long), though I am designing this one to

hold shorter panel saws 
---
or now.

Rule No. 2: High Enough to Make a Human Tripod
In general, modern tool chests are shorter than traditional ones — a trend

during the last 60 years or so. The typical explanation 
---
or this trend is that
the modern woodworker has 
---
ewer and more compact tools, hence the

shrunken box.

The problem with these stunted boxes is they are harder to use. They are
about 14" to 16" high, and when you put them on the 
---
loor, it is ridiculously
hard to bend over to 
---
etch a tool 
---
rom them. So you put them on top o
---
 your

360

THE

ANARCHIST s TOO

CHEST

34"-high workbench or table saw. Then the opening o
---
 your toolbox is about
50" 
---
rom the 
---
loor. That's a bit high, and the toolbox is now taking up valuable space on your benchtop.
Traditional tool boxes are usually about 22" to 27" high, with a lot o
---
 them
at 24" high. That height is ideal 
---
or the human 
---
orm, in my opinion. The rim
o
---
 the tool chest is slightly below the pivot point o
---
 your waist. So you bend

over and place your o
---

---
-hand on the rim o
---
 the chest to stabilize yoursel

Rule No. 5: A Depth to Match Your Reach
The depth o
---

at least in the same range. This makes sense 
---
or a lot o
---

---

---

---
or a
lot o
---

with its lid open. A deeper chest would be a pain to use.

Imagine a 56"-deep chest. Your arms would have a heck o
---

---

would be inconvenient because you'd have to strain to reach the lid, which is
angled back by several degrees when 
---

Deep chests are less than ideal in the same way that deep workbenches
are. Our arms are com

---
or a tool. Any

You might not think this is a big deal, but a
---

benches, I can assure you it is.

So now we have sketched out the shell o

the material we have. Speaking o

or
the chest? The natural inclination is to use something strong, such as oak or
maple. But tool chests in these materials aren't common. Why?

Rule No. 7: The Bottom Can Be Nailed. But Why?
So a
---

In retrospect, this wasn't a good idea. A solid panel bottom will move a
lot compared to 
---

seasonal expansion and contraction. So i
---

---

groove in the shell, which isn’t ideal. You want everything to be as tight as
possible.

There are some other good reasons to use individual boards secured by
nails. I
---

replacing an entire panel, no matter how the bottom is attached.

This is a problem. Nails and screws don’t hold as tightly into end grain
as they do into 
---
ace grain. So I wanted to introduce some glue into the joint

to help things along. O
---

when you glue long grain to end grain, the end grain will try to bust apart
the joint as it expands and contracts with the seasons.

There are several solutions to this problem. Some involve a sliding dove-

tail. Others involve screws in elongated slots. The simplest solution is to glue
and nail the trim on at the 
---
ront o
---

o
---
 the lid. This was the technique that the original builder had used. The theory here is that the glue and nails will keep the trim secure and tight up at the
miters, and the nails at the back o
---

It's an interesting theory and one that sometimes works. It sure didn’t
work

Rule No. 13: Finish, Inside ee Out
This is the easy part. The outside o
---
 a tool chest should be painted. The
modern choice is to use milk paint, which is quite durable and looks better
as it ages. We don’t have lead-based paints available, which were the paint

o
---
 choice in the pre-Industrial world.
Paint will keep your chest looking good 
---
or a long time. Any time it gets
banged up, you can renew the look with another coat o
---
 paint. A stain or
clear 
---
inish cannot be renewed as easily. Plus, paint is the most weather-,
UV- and abuse-resistant 
---
inish available. Unless you want to dunk your

chest in Plasti Dip.
On the inside o
---

---
inish. I
---

---

---
inish it, however. For that, I’d recom-

mend shellac, which will cure quickly and not leave a nasty oily smell that
can last 
---
or years in an enclosed chest like a linseed oil 
---
inish will.

Rabbet Seeks Dove 
---
or Long-term Relationship

any slight bow in either piece. That way, when you mark, cut and assemble

the joint it will pull the bow out and you will have a per
---
ect seam. It also
saves chiseling time as you will see below.

Excavating Waste
I've always sawn out my dovetail waste with a coping saw. I’m 
---
ast with a
coping saw and can get quite close to my baseline without going over.
You can chisel out your waste without sawing it. But here’s why I do
what I do: I use a coping saw because it’s easier to replace a dull coping saw
blade than it is to sharpen my chisel several times during a project.
By sawing out the waste, my chisel stays sharp much longer because I’m

using it less. And a coping saw blade lasts me 
---

Natl that Bottom
The bottom o
---
 the tool chest should be nailed to the shell, then wrapped
by the skirt moulding. This arrangement accomplishes many goals. One, it

makes the bottom boards easy to replace i
---
 they become rotted 
---
rom sitting
in water. Second, you can nail them in place in two directions: Up through
the bottom and through the base moulding. This two-way nailing scheme

am not a slavish

---
 the so-called “column orders” o
---

The “column orders” are where you apply the proportions 
---
rom classi-

cal columns to architecture and 
---
urniture. There are di
---

---

— Ionic, Corinthian ete. — that have slightly di
---

But they all come down to one basic idea:

Every structure has a base (sometimes called the plinth), a middle sec-

tion (the column, proper) and a capital (the top bit).
When you reduce the idea to this level o
---
 simplicity, I can get behind it.
Our 1928 Tudor-revival house, 
---
or example, doesn’t have a real base.

Because o

---

---

No, I didn’t raise up the house. Instead, I planted boxwoods along the 
---
ront
o
---
 the house, which concealed the lack o
---
a base and in time actually created

a 
---

---

look o
---
 the structure.

As much as the classical orders seem distant and academic ideas at times,

they o

---
er real guidelines that we can adopt or adapt. And you cannot or
should not ignore them when building a tool chest.

Fitting the Skirt
Getting the skirt boards to wrap around the carcase without splitting, bend-

ing or leaving an ugly gap might seem like a big challenge. In 
---
act, it looks
like you need to do some care
---
ul measurements to get everything lined up so
the assembled skirt just slides onto the carcase.
Truth is, you shouldn’t measure jack crap when you make the skirt.
Here are the 
---
acts: Your carcase is not entirely square. Each 
---
ace might

SKIRTS

409

even taper in or out a bit. Who knows? Measuring the carcase and then
making the skirts 
---
it those measurements is the road to 
---
ailure.
Instead, you need to 
---
ocus on 
---
itting the skirt boards one by one. This is
simple to do, doesn’t require any real measuring and produces good results.

Begin at the End
Set the chest on end and clamp a skirt board to the end o
---
 the carcase that's
propped up in the air. Center the skirt board as best you can. I
---
 it’s a little too
long, then that’s OK. I
---

---
etch another couple boards.
Once the skirt board is centered, you can set a marking gauge to scribe

your baselines. I
---

line up with the corners o
---
 the carcase.
I
---

---

you cannot mark a consistent baseline because o
---
 the shape o
---
 the carcase, I
recommend you trace the shape o
---
the carcase on the skirt board. Then wrap

the baseline around the skirt board.
Either way, you should end up with baselines that 
---
ollow the contours o
---

the carcase.

The Dust Seal
The so-called dust seal on a tool chest has an aesthetic and practical purpose.

The seal is actually a mini-skirt that wraps around the carcase near the top.
Then the lid, which also has a mini-skirt around it, sleeves over the top o
---
 the
carcase and meets the skirt below.

It sounds more complex than it really is. The result o
---
 all this tricky 
---
itting is that you have a lid that will not allow the casual dust mote to enter

your chest. Without the dust seal, even a slight warping o
---
 the top would
allow dust inside the chest, bringing in harm
---
ul salts that will absorb water

---
rom the air and pit your tools with iron oxide (rust).

The seal ensures that only the most determined specks o
---
 dust will 
---
ind
their way into your chest.

Now, there are other more advanced 
---
orms o
---
 dust seals out there. My


---
irst chest had a dust seal that was an engineering marvel. The top mini-skirt
also had a rabbet on it. So the lid and its skirt nested into the rabbet o
---
 the
skirt on the carcase. The seal works brilliantly, but the rabbet is a mite 
---
ragile

and has split o
---

---
 in a couple places 
---
rom the hard knocks o
---
 shop li
---
e.

---
ore the great slide. This is the dry-assembly, right be

ore | broke out the glue and all

t is di

The lids are too thin or move too much with the seasons. They are not

---

rip out their own hinges with normal use. They won't work with a lock over
the long term.

And they crack beneath your bony or ample buttocks.
When I set out to make the lid 
---

---

or sparest thing around. But because o

The trick is to use lightweight wood (natch) but to exploit its strengths

and use joinery that is bulletproo
---
. So the lid should be 7/8"-thick pine —
perhaps even a bit thicker i

lat.
For joinery, I used through-tenons at the corners o

but it is raised.

---

The di

construction. To make the panel interlock with the rails and stiles, you plow
a groove in the panel. Instead o

When the lid is assembled, the lower tongue o

groove in the 
---

Lid Design
As with all 
---
rame constructions, you should select the straightest quartersawn or ri
---
t-sawn stock possible to make the rails and stiles. Not only does
this look good, but the stock will move less and is unlikely to warp.
When selecting my material 
---
or rails and stiles, I am per
---

to selectively cut up really wide or choice boards to pick out just the piece
I need 
---

---

It’s all about timing. Lining up the slots on the screw heads is called “timing” the screws
so they all stop in the same place. Yes, it is totally anal retentive.

A
---
ter you cut a mortise, install the lea
---
 into it so you don't get the hinges
mixed up while installing them. Sometimes the hinges vary 
---
rom piece to
piece.

Installing hinge screws takes time to do right. You need to drill a pilot
hole that is the right size 
---
or the screw and it needs to be centered. Some
people sight the hole by eye. Some mark it with an awl then drill the hole.
Others use a special sel
---
-centering drill bit.
I've used all these techniques and they are varying degrees o
---
 unreliable
and expensive (the special sel
---
-centering bits tend to break).

I use a sel
---
-centering punch to locate the pilot holes. This 1s an inexpensive tool available at any hardware store. You do need to learn to use it prop-

erly. Some people tap them with a hammer to drive the centered pin into the
wood. Other use 
---
inger pressure. I recommend 
---
inger pressure because the
pin is less likely to shi
---
t o
---

---
-center.

hen you start dividing up your space in your tool chest, one
o
---

---
irst questions you need to answer is where you are go-

ing to store the big iron. The longest tools — particularly your
handsaws and your jointer plane — have to be dealt with 
---
irst. Don’t try to
sneak them in a
---
ter the 
---
act.

The question o
---

---
orward
to answer: Put it in the bottom o
---
 the chest. Most tool chests have a well in
the bottom 
---
or the bench planes and joinery planes. The thornier question,
really, is where to put your big Saws.

Some English joiners would build a till on the underside o
---
 the chest’s lid


---
or the saws. This works (I have a till like this on my 
---
irst chest). The only
downside to this approach is that you have to have a lot o
---
 clearance to the
sides o
---
 your chest to pull these saws out without hitting something. Depending on my shop set-up, I've run into walls, benches, cabinets and other
tool racks. Or you can secure a 
---
ew saws to the underside o
---
 the lid with
toggles that hold the handles. These eat up “airspace” at the top o
---
 the chest.

The third option is to store the saws in a “saw till” in the bottom o
---
 the
tool chest — a rack that is usually up against the 
---
ront wall o
---
 the chest. This
location is convenient and it doesn’t require you to have any extra clearance
to the sides o
---
your chest to whip things out.

The downside to storing them on the 
---
loor o
---
 the chest is that you eat up
some valuable 
---
loor space, plus some valuable airspace above the saws.

You can reduce your guilt about gobbling up so much space by shoehorning in some other tools in the same area as your saws. Typically, joiners
would try to incorporate a chisel rack in or around the saw till 
---
or bench

chisels, mortise chisels and gouges. Another typical approach was to add a
rack at the 
---
ront that would hold try squares. Still another approach was to

hang the joinery backsaws vertically amongst the handsaws and ripsaws.

So there are a lot o
---
 valid paths to take with your sawtill. Here's what I
decided to do: A
---
ter living with an English sawtill on the lid o
---
 my last chest,

This is a test. To ensure my saws were easy to grab and remove, | mocked up the two
upright pieces | was going to use

The till I chose to build is traditional, and it works well. The till is simply

two upright pieces o
---

the slots. The other interesting 
---

---

pieces, and it makes it a little easier to grab the saw you want.

splitting with the seasons.

he last signi

a typical modern, and make your trays slide le

So what is this historical model? Typically you will have two or three
trays, depending on how tall your chest is. Small chests have two trays. Tall

chests, three. The bottom tray is deep. The top tray (or trays) are shallow.
When I divided up the airspace above my planes I could 
---
it two small

trays that are 2-3/4" high and 9" wide, including the bottoms. The large tray
has a 5-1/2" height and a 9" width.
Those are outside dimensions o
---
 the trays. The interior dimensions depend on how thin you can manage to make your components. Oh, and you
want the trays to be lightweight, strong and hard-wearing.
To balance these competing interests, you need to be able to tweak the

joinery and the types o
---
 materials you are using to make your tills.
To make the trays lightweight and strong, make the walls 
---
rom thin
pine that is dovetailed at the corners. Pine that’s 1/2" thick should be strong
enough. To make the trays hard-wearing, make the bottoms using thin slips

o
---
 white oak nailed to the bottoms o
---
 the trays. I used 1/4"-thick oak 
---
or the
small trays and 1/2"-thick oak 
---
or the big tray.
By nailing the bottoms to the trays, you accomplish several goals: You

create more storage space than i
---
 you put the bottom pieces in a groove in

Tray Construction
A
---

the chair red, then you paint it black. Then you let nature run its course. The

black paint gets rubbed through, and the red emerges in the areas that see
the most wear. This looks great.

---
ormity, the dead level only minimizes costs and increased output.
“T
---
 we are going not only to acquiesce but to

then we shall get standardization — and deserve it.

“T
---

— Charles H. Hayward, The Woodworker, April 1940

hen a tree is cut down

by chainsaws

or a storm,

there's a

strange period o
---
 time when its leaves are still vibrant, green

and supple, like the tree hasn’t quite 
---
igured out that it’s al-

ready dead.
It can take days 
---
or the news o
---
 the death to make it to the 
---
ar reaches o
---

the plant, and then the leaves droop, crinkle and 
---
all o
---

---
 one by one until all
that is le
---
t is the rotting skeleton o
---
 branches.

I think it’s a bold but 
---
air statement to say that the cra
---
t o
---
 woodworking
was cut down by the a
---
termath o
---
 World War II. The global reconstruction
in Europe and the mass mechanization required to do it quickly girdled the
bole o
---
 the woodworking cra
---
t. Chisels were put away. Moulding planes

were burned. Saws were allowed to rust.
To be sure, hand woodworking had been in decline (especially in the
Americas) since the advent o
---
 the Industrial Revolution in the 19th cen-

tury. But while Americans embraced industry, the English and the Europeans kept handcra
---
t alive with the help o
---
 the medieval guild system. Be
---
ore
World War IIL, there were still lots o
---
 quality hand tools being made and used
in shops. Even the in
---
ill plane maker Norris was hanging on until the war.

THE

456

ANARCHIST’S

TOOL

CHEST

Up until 1945, hand tools were still the norm in the home. Power tools
were too expensive and were reserved 
---
or industry. Heck, just look at Stanley’s production history to see this whole thing play out. Up until the war,
Stanley was still making a 
---
ull range o
---
 high-quality planes and chisels. A
---
ter
the war, things took a nosedive in New Britain, Conn. Today tool collectors

draw a bright line between tools made be
---
ore World War II and those made
a
---
ter.

A
---
ter the war, the world turned upside down. Rebuilding the decimated
cities and towns across Europe required new ways o
---
 building (enter the
32mm system o
---
 making cabinets). New materials (PVA glue). And mecha-

nization to allow it to be done quickly and cheaply.

And it was the same mechanization that helped 
---
actories crank out electric tools, making them inexpensive enough 
---
or the home hobbyist.

Stu
---

---
 like this doesn’t change the world overnight. Some people — like my
hero Charles H. Hayward — saw it coming early on and remarked upon it
many times in his editorials in The Woodworker magazine.
But, like the death o
---
 a tree, it took more than 60 years 
---
or the news to

reach every corner o
---
 the globe, including one remarkable shop in Sunbury,
Ohio.
When I started at Popular Woodworking, one o
---
 my 
---
irst assignments took

me to the shop o
---
 Troy Sexton, a pro
---
essional cabinetmaker who builds 
---
urniture in a spacious shop he constructed on his 
---
arm outside Columbus, Ohio.

I spent many years working on stories 
---
or our magazine with Troy, who
built 
---
urniture alone in his shop, but somehow managed the output o
---
 three

people. Troy is impossibly 
---
ast. When I drove up to work with him on an article 
---
or the magazine, he would start a project when I arrived at about 8:30

a.m. By lunchtime, the project was all but 
---
inished, except 
---
or some sanding
and detail work.
His secret? Well, it isn’t really a secret. Troy is just one o
---
 the smartest
and 
---
astest woodworkers I've ever met. He purchases good tools and keeps

CHRISTOPHER

SCHWARZ

457

many o
---
 them set 
---
or speci
---
ic operations. Here’s a small sample o
---
 his genius:
He has a small power planer with sharp blades that is set 
---
or exactly 3/4". He
roughs out all his stock on his big machines and passes everything through
this small planer, which leaves a great 
---
inish and ensures everything is per-


---
ectly sized.
He has a small table saw set up 
---
or cutting only 
---
ace cheeks o
---
 tenons. He
has a second saw bolted to that one 
---
or cutting edge cheeks. And a small jig
that drops onto the 
---
ence to make the haunches.

He has small router tables set up to make matching cope-and-stick pro
---
iles that are always bang-on (he rein
---
orces these joints with loose tenons).

And his dovetail system still boggles my mind.
I know what you must be thinking: What the hell is going on here? |
thought this book was about promoting hand work. Well, I am all about
hand work. I use only a 
---
ew machines (
---
ewer every year). But this particular

story involves machines deeply because every choice we make in the cra
---
t
involves mechanization.

Troy does not mess around. He can do anything with any tool, whether
powered by man or electron. He souped up several old handplanes to an
extreme degree to try to produce period sur
---
ace 
---
inishes. He could make any
tool obey his whim because he had a complete mastery o
---
 the way that wood

and steel interact.
But Troy decided to compete with the machines on their own tur
---
. He
tried to become a one-man


---
actory. He succeeded in that. But the most e
---
-


---
icient one-man 
---
actory cannot compete with a 
---
actory driven by a hundred

men.

So no matter what Troy did, the prices 
---
or work (good or otherwise)
declined. Ikea’s well-designed kitchens and Value City’s cheap junk made it

impossible to survive and live well.
So Troy struggled with his business. And that’s nuts. He should have had

more business than he could handle. But like many pro
---
essional cabinetmak-

THE

458

ANARCHIST Ss" hO

Er ST
OAGr

ers I know, it became harder and harder 
---
or him to make ends meet every
year. His cabinetmaking business is now a shadow o
---
 its 
---
ormer sel
---
, and
Troy spends more o
---
 his time working 
---
or his wi
---
e's success
---
ul tax business.

Who is to blame 
---
or this? We are.
We have become a culture that is obsessed with price more than any oth-

er attribute o
---
 the things we buy. It doesn’t matter i
---
 the item is ugly, poorly
made or constructed o
---
 materials that cannot be recycled. All that matters is
i
---
 the price is low enough.
Because the price o
---
 our household objects has hit rock bottom, i
---
 an item
breaks or starts to look dated, we can throw it away and buy something else.

For the 
---
irst time in human history, manu
---
actured 
---
urniture is shockingly
inexpensive.
So it’s no wonder that artisans are exiting the cra
---
t. It’s di
---

---
icult to compete against 
---
urniture that costs less than what you pay 
---
or your raw materi-

als.
Lesson: People cannot imitate machines and de
---
eat them.
Don't believe me? There is little doubt that the pro
---
essional class o
---
 artisans is shrinking. There were 140,000 cabinetmakers and bench carpenters
in the United States in 1999. In 2003, there were 126,350. As o
---
 2009, that
number had shrunk to 99,870, according to the Bureau o
---
 Labor Statistics.

And those declining numbers are the overarching reason I wrote “The

Anarchist’s Tool Chest.” Yeah, I know it is arguable that this book is a bellybutton-gazing text about how handcra
---
t is in decline and ... cue the whining

and hand-wringing.

But the truth is, I don’t give a crap about personal sel
---
-actualization. I
don't care i
---
 you 
---
ind peace in your smoothing plane. I don’t care i
---
 you 
---
ind

your power animal.

What I care about is the cra
---
t o
---
 woodworking, which is closer to extinction now than at any other time in the history o
---
 the human race. How do
we save it?

CHRISTOPHER

SCHWARZ

459

I'm so glad you asked.
It is our duty as amateurs to pick up the mantle o
---
 the cra
---
t o
---
 woodwork-

ing 
---
rom the pro
---
essionals, who have been carrying this heavy burden 
---
or us


---
or generation a
---
ter generation. It is our turn to preserve the design, joinery
and 
---
inishing skills that are now being trans
---
erred to CNC machinery.
How can we do this? Part o
---
 this is done by sustaining the toolmakers

who make tools that actually 
---
unction. Part o
---
 this is done by preserving the
written woodworking knowledge about how to build stu
---

---
 without computers or automation.

But those two tasks above are the easy things to do. The hard part is the
important part. We have to acquire, store and redistribute the actual hand

skills. But you can’t deposit those into a book, a video or a computer.
There is only one place that hard-won hand skills can be stored then ac-

cessed by 
---
uture generations: that is in your hands and your heart.
By absorbing and practicing these skills we can ensure they will not be

lost — as long as we are willing to teach them 
---
reely to younger woodworkers. For most o
---
 human history, the important stu
---

---
 about working wood was

never written down or shared outside a tight-knit group o
---
 pro
---
essionals.
Most o
---
 their knowledge is gone. Read George Sturt’s “The Wheelwright’s
Shop” 
---
or a 
---
irst-hand example o
---
 this. I ask you to share what you know
with anyone who will listen. Write a book. Start a blog.

But those tasks — di
---

---
icult as they are — are not all I am asking o
---
you.
I
---
 you honestly want to preserve the cra
---
t as more than an academic curiosity — perhaps to lay the groundwork 
---
or a cra
---
t revival, then try this: Consider living more like an 18th-century artisan and less like a 21st-century

mega-consumer. This path isn’t chock-
---
ull o
---
 
---
un. I know because I’m on it.
During the last decade, my priorities have shi
---
ted to the point where now I:
¢ Buy things that are well-made by skilled people who sell them 
---
or a 
---
air
price.

Decline to purchase cheap goods that are designed to be discarded.

THE

460

ANARCHIST2S) 1 OOteeGn

Est

¢ Whenever possible, make exactly what I need, instead o
---
 buying something that will su
---

---
ice 
---
or now.

For me, this behavior has resulted in my questioning the institutions that

encourage waste
---
ul consumption, particularly large corporations and the
governments in service to them. These institutions are the opposite o
---
 indi-

vidual enterprise, and I worry about the way they work because I think they
endanger our cra
---
t.

I call this practical skepticism, but its more proper name is “anarchism.”
So be it.
By living like an artisan and encouraging others to do likewise, it's my
sincerest hope that it will some day encourage a renaissance in the cra
---
t that

is similar to what has occurred in other nearly lost pre-industrial pro
---
essions,
such as small-scale brewing, micro-
---
arming and even cheese-making.
I
---
 a woodworking renaissance ever occurs, then you and I will have laid
the groundwork 
---
or a 
---
uture generation o
---
 cra
---
tsmen. With our help, they

will have the hand skills we have taught them. They will have access to the
books to help them continue to learn the cra
---
t. They will have a stout chest


---
illed with quality tools that are sharp. They will be ready to build 
---
urniture
that lasts 
---
orever and is better than anything a computer could construct.

Or, to end this book on a more “writerly” note and bring the story 
---
ull
circle so my college newswriting pro
---
essor will approve: We will have gathered the acorns 
---
rom around the 
---
allen tree and spread them throughout the

land in the hope that some o
---
 them will take root to replace what was lost
a
---
ter the war.

Appendix B: Vintage Tool Dealers
could never compile a comprehensive list o
---
 tool dealers. There are too

many honorable people out there selling tools 
---
or both collectors and
users. My 
---
irst and best recommendation is to join the tool-collecting
clubs and attend their meetings. There is no better way to see thousands o
---


tools in one place and become educated in their di
---

---
erences.
Plus, i
---
 you really are a user and not a collector, you'll 
---
ind many items
priced 
---
or you.

Mid-West Tool Collectors Association (U-WTCA)
mwtca.org

This is the world’s largest tool-collecting organization. It’s inexpensive to
become a member. And the bene
---
its are tremendous. The organization has

about 40 meeting across the country each year. And these meetings are great
places to learn about, sell and buy old tools. Another good reason to join:

You get reprints o
---
 awesome vintage tool catalogs.

Early American Industries Association (EAIA)
eaiain
---
o.org

The EAIA is like the Mid-West group, but its meetings and publications
are more highbrow. (Note: I belong to — and avidly support — both). The
organization's quarterly magazine, The Chronicle, alone is worth the price o
---

admission. EAIA also has annual and smaller meetings that have both selling

and educational components. Honestly, I think you should join both.

Other Ways to Buy Toots
I’m not a joiner. In 
---
act, the above organizations are the only two “clubs” I

belong to. I don’t even belong to any societies o
---
 journalists (
---
unctional alco-

---

cleaning products.

not have to join an organization 
---
or a 
---
ew bucks a year. You can troll eBay —

hell you can have it. eBay is 
---

 you want to buy good vintage tools, then you need to buy
